لتثمير المال وذلك يتحقق بالرأي الكامل والشفقة الوافرة فلا بد من اجتماعهما والوجود في كل واحد منهما أحدهما، وأما الإجارة ففيها روايتان فرواية القدوري أنه يؤجره، وفي رواية الجامع الصغير أنه لا يجوز أن يؤجره ؛ كذا ذكره في الكراهية وهو الأصح. وجه الأول أنه يكون له وبه عرف أن الدار التي هو فيها وكذا البستان لا يكون له بالأولى. قوله : (وفي الجامع يصح
Slavery does not prevent marriage initially nor in continuance, so it is not a necessary consequence of the ruling of her slavery to negate the marriage. And if a [foundling] reaches puberty and marries a woman, then later acknowledges that he is a slave for so-and-so, and his wife has a dowry owed to her, that dowry is binding upon him; his acknowledgment is not accepted to invalidate it because it is a debt whose obligation became apparent, so he is suspect in his acknowledgment. Similarly, if he takes a loan, or sells something, or guarantees, or gifts, or gives charity and hands it over, or makes a mudabbar, or writes a kitaba, or frees a slave, and then acknowledges that he is a slave of so-and-so, his acknowledgment is not accepted in that because it is invalidating something from a suspect [person]. This is as in Fath al-Qadir and al-Khaniya. And she added: If the person to whom slavery is acknowledged frees her [the wife] while she is under a husband, she does not have the option of freedom. If the husband divorced her once and she acknowledged slavery, her divorce becomes two; the husband does not have [remaining] over her except one divorce. If he divorced her twice and then she acknowledged slavery, he has the right to take her back. Similarly, the ruling of the woman in her waiting period: if she acknowledges slavery after having two menses, he has the right to take her back in the third menses. End. And similarly mentioned in al-Muhit, and he added: If a foundling makes a mudabbar slave, and then acknowledges belonging to another as a slave, then dies, the mudabbar is freed from one-third of his [the foundling's] estate, and he is to work to earn two-thirds of his value for his [true] master. Because the one acknowledging slavery remains free in respect of the mudabbar, and he died and has no property other than the mudabbar, so he works to earn two-thirds of his value for his master, because he [the foundling] acknowledges that for his master. And if his master frees him, the mudabbar remains in his state except that his service is for the master, and his earning after the death of the foundling is for the master, because the mudabbar acknowledges service and earning for the foundling, and he [the foundling] acknowledges that for his master, so it becomes like one who acknowledges for the person acknowledged to. End. And he mentioned it in al-Muhit from the Book of Iqrars, and added in the chapter of acknowledgment of slavery: What she gives birth to before or after [the acknowledgment] in less than six months is free, because it is known that its conception preceded the acknowledgment, so it is not accepted to invalidate its freedom. If she gives birth after more [than six months], then according to Abu Yusuf it is a slave, contrary to Muhammad, because the husband acquired the right of free children, so this right is not invalidated by her acknowledgment. And it is mentioned in al-Ziyadat: If the husband divorced her with two divorces, and he knows of her acknowledgment, he has the right of return over her; if he knows [she is free], he does not have [that right]. And it is mentioned in al-Jami': he does not have [the right] whether he knows or not. It is said: What is mentioned in al-Jami' is qiyas, and what is mentioned in al-Ziyadat is istihsan, and it is sahih. And if a person of unknown freedom buys a slave, frees him, then acknowledges [himself] as a slave, and the freed one denies [it], and the acknowledger has an adult son who also denies, then the acknowledger becomes a slave and the freed one remains free. If the freed one dies leaving property and agnates, his property goes to his agnates. If he has no heir other than the one who freed him, his property goes to the person acknowledged to. If the deceased has a daughter, half goes to her and half to the person acknowledged to. If this freed one commits an offense, his blood-money is upon him. If an offense is committed against him, it is like an offense against a slave, and he is like a slave in testimony, because his freedom is established by the apparent [state] not by evidence, so he is suitable for repelling [claims] not for acquiring rights. And if the person acknowledged to frees the acknowledger, then the first freed one dies without agnates, his inheritance goes to the person acknowledged to. End. And in it also: If the married woman acknowledges slavery, and the husband gave her the mahr before her acknowledgment, he is discharged; after her acknowledgment, he is not discharged, because the mahr becomes the property of the person acknowledged to. End. And it indicates that she is a slave in respect of division [of nights] in marriage, and it is appropriate that her delivery to the husband is like the delivery of free women; so the person acknowledged to does not have the right to employ her or prevent her from living with the husband because of the harm involved, so she deserves maintenance without being placed in the marital home. And al-Muhit restricted it with the denial of the freed one, and did not clarify its implication. And in Talkhis al-Jami': if the freed one confirms his master in his acknowledgment of slavery, his manumission is invalidated because the prevention is in his interest, since wala' can be invalidated, as evidence by the freed woman who apostatizes and is taken captive. And in al-TatarKhaniyya: If he acknowledges that he is a slave, it is not accepted to invalidate anything that he has done except marriage. Because when he acknowledges slavery, he has claimed that the marriage was not valid due to lack of permission from the one he claims to be his master, so he must be held to his claim, unlike the woman if she acknowledges slavery: her marriage is not invalidated. End. His statement: (And if property is found with him, it is his) based on the apparent [state]. And an objection is raised that it is sufficient for repelling [claims] not for acquiring rights; if ownership is established for the foundling by this apparent [state], that would mean the apparent is a proof. We say: This apparent [state] is used to repel the claim of another. Moreover, the apparent [state] is that properties are in the hands of their owners. Similarly, the apparent [state] indicates that the one who placed it with him placed it so that he would spend on him. It is general, including whether the property is tied on him or an animal on which he is tied; if the foundling is found on an animal, it is his. It is reported that a foundling girl was found in Baghdad, and at her chest was a scroll containing: 'This is the daughter of Shuqi, Shaqiyya bint al-Tabahja al-Qaliyya, and with her a thousand Ja'fari dinars to buy an Indian slave-girl, and this is the punishment for the one who did not marry off his daughter when she was older' and in another narration 'when she was small'. This as in al-Jawhara. And in it: If the property is placed near him, they do not rule that it is his; it is a lost item. End. It is clear that dirhams and dinars placed on him are his because they fall under 'property with him'. It should be that dirhams above his bedding or under it are his like his clothing, mattress, and blanket, unlike if they are buried under it. I have not seen [this ruling] just as I have not seen the ruling on if he is found alone in a house or garden: do they belong to him? In Rawd al-Shafi'iyya it is stated that the house is his, and concerning the garden there are two opinions. The author did not mention the finder spending from his own property on him. In al-Hidaya: He [the finder] then spends on him by order of the judge because it is lost property, and the judge has authority to spend similar on it. And it is said: he spends without the judge's order because it is for the foundling apparently, and he has authority to spend and buy necessities like food and clothing because it is part of spending. End. Similarly, other than the finder, by the judge's order, and his word is accepted regarding expenses similar to it. It is appropriate that the permission of the judge be conditional if possible; otherwise, having witnesses is sufficient. His statement: (And the finder does not have over him marriage, sale, or hire) As for marriage, due to the absence of the cause of authority such as kinship, ownership, or sovereign power. As for his disposal of his property through sale etc., it is by analogy with the mother, because the authority to dispose is to increase the property, and that is achieved by complete opinion and abundant compassion; both must be present, and for each of them only one is found. As for hire, there are two narrations: according to al-Quduri, he may hire him out; in the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, he may not hire him out. So it is mentioned in al-Karahiyya, and it is the most sound. The argument for the first is that it would be his, and custom [shows] that the house and garden are not his. His statement: (And in al-Jami' it is valid...).
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the rulings concerning a foundling (laqit) who later acknowledges being a slave, and the property found with him. The text begins by establishing a key principle: slavery does not negate marriage either initially or in continuity, so a foundling who marries and then claims to be a slave cannot use that claim to invalidate his marriage or the dowry obligations. Similarly, his acknowledgment is not accepted to nullify debts, sales, gifts, or manumissions he performed prior to the claim, because he is considered 'muttaham' (suspect) in his attempt to revoke rights of others.

The text then moves to specific scenarios. If a woman (the wife) acknowledges slavery after her husband has divorced her once, the divorce count increases to two, affecting his ability to take her back. The same applies during her 'idda: if she acknowledges slavery after two menses, the husband can still revoke in the third. This shows that her acknowledgment has legal consequences but does not retroactively destroy her freedom-based rights.

Regarding the mudabbar (a slave whose manumission is deferred until the master's death): if a foundling who has made a mudabbar later acknowledges being a slave and dies, the mudabbar is freed from one-third of the foundling's estate (since he had no other property), and must work to pay two-thirds of his value to his actual master. This is a form of istihsan balancing the interests of the mudabbar and the true master.

For children born to a woman who acknowledges slavery: if born within six months, the child is free because its conception preceded the claim; if after six months, Abu Yusuf holds the child is a slave (since the father's right to free children is established), while Muhammad holds it is free. The text indicates the correct position is the one in al-Jami' (qiyas) that the husband has no right of return if he knew of her claim, though al-Ziyadat adopts istihsan allowing him to take her back if he did not know.

The case of a person of unknown freedom who buys a slave, frees him, then claims to be a slave himself: his claim is accepted regarding his own status, but the freed slave remains free, and matters of inheritance and liability are complicated by the partial acknowledgment. The freed slave is considered free in appearance for defending against claims but not for acquiring rights like inheritance.

The second part of the passage deals with property found with the foundling. The principle is 'اعتباراً للظاهر' (consideration of the apparent), meaning that property physically on the foundling (tied on him, or he on an animal) is presumed to be his. This presumption is sufficient to repel others' claims but not to establish full ownership in all contexts. The text distinguishes between property on his person, near him (which is lost property), and buried under his bed (not his). If found in a house or garden alone, the house is his but the garden is disputed among Shafi'is.

Finally, the finder's authority over the foundling is limited: he cannot marry him off, sell his property, or hire him out by his own authority. He must act through the qadi. For hiring, there is a difference of narration: al-Quduri allows it, while al-Jami' al-Saghir does not; the latter is more sound. The finder is compared to the mother, who lacks full legal capacity for such actions.

Overall, this passage demonstrates the Hanafi approach to balancing conflicting interests—the foundling's apparent freedom, the rights of those he may have injured, and the need for judicial oversight—using principles like 'muttaham', 'istihsan', and 'zahir'.

Key Terms
اللقيط (al-laqīṭ): A foundling, an abandoned child whose lineage is unknown.
المقر له (al-muqarr lahu): The person to whom the foundling acknowledges he belongs as a slave.
المقر (al-muqirr): The person making the acknowledgment of being a slave.
متهم (muttaham): A person suspected of making a claim that serves his own interest, especially in revoking obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason a foundling's later acknowledgment of slavery does not invalidate his prior sale of property?
A) Because the foundling had no right to sell as a slave.
B) Because the foundling is considered suspect (muttaham) in his claim, and his prior act was valid under apparent freedom.
C) Because the buyer knew the foundling might be a slave.
D) Because the sale was conducted with a judge's permission.

Key Takeaway
A foundling's confession of slavery is accepted for his own status but cannot retroactively invalidate prior legal acts that involved the rights of others.

Foundling's Admission of Slavery and Its Effect on Marriage
Intra-Hanafi dispute on foundling's admission of slavery affecting marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effect of a foundling's admission of slavery on his marriage versus other actions, and explain the Hanafi reasoning based on the master's rights and the involvement of a third party.

Core Idea
A foundling's admission of slavery invalidates his own acts except marriage—because marriage involves the right of the spouse.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: if a foundling (laqit) who was married later admits to being a slave, does his admission invalidate his marriage? The text navigates between different Hanafi authorities, ultimately distinguishing marriage from other actions due to the involvement of another party's rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling from al-Tatarkhaniyya
The foundling's admission does not invalidate marriage but does invalidate other actions like emancipation.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Contrast with the case in Talkhis al-Jami'
The master's admission, confirmed by the freed slave, invalidates emancipation because clientage can be annulled.
This is a different scenario—master's admission vs. foundling's own admission.
3
Analyze the rationale
Why marriage is treated differently: it involves the spouse's right, so the foundling's unilateral admission cannot nullify it.
The spouse is a third party not bound by the admission.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical outcome: the foundling remains married, but in other matters (e.g., emancipation) he may be considered a slave if the master confirms.
The distinction avoids harming the spouse.
5
Reconcile the sources
The Hanafi position is settled in al-Tatarkhaniyya, which prioritizes the protection of marriage contracts.
This resolves the apparent conflict between texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النكاح، وينبغي أن يكون تسليمها للزوج كتسليم الحرائر فلا يملك المقر له استخدامها ومنعها من السكنى مع الزوج لما فيه من الإضرار فتستحق النفقة بلا تبوئة وقيد في المحيط بجحد العتيق ولم يصرح بمفهومه، وصرح في تلخيص الجامع بأنه لو صدق العتيق مولاه في إقراره بالرق يبطل عتقه لأن المنع لحقه إذ الولاء يقبل البطلان بدليل العتيقة ترتد فتسبى. وفي التتارخانية : إذا أقر أنه عبد لا يصدق على إبطال شيء كان فعله إلا النكاح لأنه لما أقر بالرق فقد زعم أن النكاح لم يصح لعدم إذن من يزعم أنه مولاه فيجب أن يؤاخذ بزعمه بخلاف المرأة لو أقرت بالرق لا يبطل نكاحها اهـ .
Marriage: Her delivery to the husband should be like the delivery of free women, so the person to whom she is assigned does not have the right to employ her or prevent her from living with her husband because of harm; thus she deserves maintenance without the requirement of cohabitation. In al-Muhit, it is conditioned on the denial of the freed slave, but its implication is not explicitly stated. In Talkhis al-Jami', it is explicitly stated that if the freed slave confirms his master's admission of slavery, his emancipation is invalidated because the prohibition is against him, since clientage is subject to nullification, as evidenced by the freed female slave who apostatizes and is taken captive. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: If he admits he is a slave, he is not believed to invalidate anything he has done except marriage; because when he admitted to slavery, he claimed that the marriage was invalid due to lack of permission from the one he claims is his master, so he must be held to his claim, contrary to a woman who admits to slavery: her marriage is not invalidated.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding a foundling girl who has been given in marriage. It addresses the situation where someone claims ownership over her (as a slave) after the marriage. The Hanafi position is that she should be handed over to her husband like a free woman, and the claimant does not have the right to use her as a slave or prevent her from living with her husband, as this would cause harm. She is entitled to maintenance without the requirement of her husband providing a separate dwelling (tabwi'). However, a condition is noted in al-Muhit: the freed slave (the claimant) must deny the marriage, but the text does not explicitly state the logical implication. Further, in Talkhis al-Jami', it is stated that if the freed slave confirms his master's admission (that he was never freed), the emancipation is invalidated, because the prohibition against enslaving him is based on the false claim of freedom, and clientage (wala') can be nullified, as seen when a freed female slave apostatizes and is taken captive. Then, from al-Tatarkhaniyya, a nuanced ruling: if the foundling himself admits to being a slave, this admission invalidates his own actions except for marriage. Because by admitting slavery, he claims the marriage was invalid for lack of master's permission, so he is held to that claim. But if a woman admits to slavery, her marriage is not invalidated by her admission. The difference is that a woman's admission affects only herself, whereas a foundling's admission affects the master's rights as well.

Key Terms
اللقيط (al-laqīṭ): A foundling, an abandoned child of unknown parentage.
المقر له (al-muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor the foundling is claimed or assigned (the one who asserts ownership).
العتيق (al-'atīq): A freed slave; here, the foundling who was previously emancipated by the claimant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Emancipation is also valid.
B) Emancipation is invalidated if the master confirms the claim.
C) Emancipation is unaffected by the admission.
D) Emancipation is always invalidated regardless of the master's confirmation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a foundling's admission of slavery does not invalidate his marriage because marriage involves the spouse's rights, but his admission may affect his own status in other matters like emancipation.

Luqatah — Found Property

Property Found with the Foundling: The Rule of Al-Zahir
Clarifies that money found on or near the foundling belongs to him, with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on property found with a foundling and apply the distinction between property on the foundling's person and property nearby.

Core Idea
Apparent circumstances (zahir) presume ownership of found property for the foundling, but only if it is physically with him.

When a foundling (lost child) is discovered, any property found with him raises a legal question: who owns it? The Hanafi school answers based on apparent circumstances (al-zahir), a principle that allows practical decisions in the absence of certainty. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its basis, and its specific applications, including the distinction between property on the child's person and property merely nearby.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Recognize that the passage addresses a foundling (laqit) with property. The key is to determine ownership of that property.
The foundling is a lost minor; the property is any item of value.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The ruling is: property found with the foundling belongs to him. This is based on the principle of al-zahir (apparent circumstances).
This is not an absolute proof of ownership but a legal presumption for practical purposes.
3
Understand the basis: al-zahir
The text uses 'اعتباراً للظاهر' (consideration of the apparent). The presumption is that property in someone's possession is theirs, and that whoever placed it with the foundling intended it for his expenses.
The text addresses an objection that zahir only suffices for defense, not for establishing ownership. The Hanafi response is that it repels claims of others.
4
Apply the key distinction
Distinguish between property that is 'with him' (e.g., tied to his person, on his clothing, under his bedding, or he is on an animal) and property that is 'near him' (placed on the ground, buried). The former is his; the latter is luqata (lost property).
Examples: dirhams on his person or under his bedding are his; buried items or items on the ground are not.
5
Examine scope and procedure
The rule extends to animals he is on (they belong to him) and, according to Rawd al-Shafi'iyya, houses he is found in alone. For spending, the finder should ideally get a judge's order, but witnessing suffices if impossible.
The commentator notes that if property is found alone in a house, it belongs to the foundling; for a garden, there are two opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن وجد معه مال فهو له) اعتباراً للظاهر وأورد عليه أن يكفي للدفع لا للاستحقاق، فلو ثبت الملك للقيط بهذا الظاهر كان الظاهر مثبتاً. قلنا: يدفع بهذا الظاهر دعوى الغير، ثم الظاهر أن تكون الأملاك في يد الملاك، وكذا الظاهر يدل على أن من وضعه معه إنما وضعه لينفق عليه. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان المال مشدوداً عليه أو دابة هو مشدود عليها وإن وجد اللقيط على دابة فهي له وحكي أن لقيطة وجدت ببغداد وعند صدرها رق منشور فيه هذه بنت شقي وشقية بنت الطباهجة والقلية ومعها ألف دينار جعفرية يشتري بها جارية هندية وهذا جزاء من لم يزوج بنته وهي كبيرة وفي رواية وهي صغيرة؛ كذا في الجوهرة. وفيها : لو كان المال موضوعاً بقربه لم يحكموا له به ويكون لقطة اهـ. ولا يخفى أن الدراهم والدنانير الموضوعة عليه له لدخولها تحت قولهم . معه مال. وينبغي أن تكون الدراهم التي فوق فراشه أو تحته له كلباسه ومهاده ودثاره بخلاف ما إذا كان مدفوناً تحته ولم أره كما لم أر حكم ما إذا وجد في دار فيها وحده أو بستان هل يكونان له؟ وصرح في روض الشافعية بأن الدار له وفي البستان وجهان. ولم يذكر المصنف إنفاق الملتقط عليه من ماله قال في الهداية : ثم يصرفه الواجد إليه بأمر القاضي لأنه مال ضائع وللقاضي ولاية صرف مثله إليه، وقيل يصرفه بغير أمر القاضي لأنه للقيط ظاهراً وله ولاية الإنفاق وشراء ما بد منه كالطعام والكسوة لأنه من الإنفاق .اهـ. وكذا الغير الواجد بأمر القاضي والقول قوله في نفقة مثله، وينبغي أن يشترط إذن القاضي إن أمكن وإلا يكفي الإشهاد.
His saying: (And if property is found with him, it belongs to him) based on apparent circumstances. It was objected that this suffices for defense [against claims] but not for establishing ownership; if ownership were established for the foundling by this apparent circumstance, then the apparent circumstance would be a proof. We say: By this apparent circumstance, the claim of others is repelled. Additionally, the apparent circumstance is that properties are in the hands of their owners; likewise, the apparent circumstance indicates that whoever placed it with him only placed it for his expenses. He [the author] gave it absolutely, so it includes if the property is tied to him or an animal he is tied to; if the foundling is found on an animal, it belongs to him. It is narrated that a foundling girl was found in Baghdad, and on her chest was an open document stating: 'This is the daughter of Shaqi, and Shaqiya bint al-Tabahijjah and al-Qaliyya, and with her a thousand Ja'fari dinars with which to buy an Indian slave-girl, and this is the punishment for one who does not marry off his daughter when she is old (and in another narration, when she is young).' This is in al-Jawhara. In it: If the property was placed near him, they do not rule that it belongs to him; it is considered a lost item. It is obvious that dirhams and dinars placed on him belong to him, as they fall under 'property with him'. Dirhams that are on his bedding or under it should belong to him, like his clothing, bedding, and cover, unlike if they were buried under him. I have not seen a ruling on whether a house or garden in which he is found alone belongs to him. In Rawd al-Shafi'iyya, it is explicitly stated that the house belongs to him; for the garden, there are two opinions. The author [al-Kanz] did not mention the guardian's spending from his own money. In al-Hidaya: Then the finder spends on him with the judge's order because it is lost property, and the judge has authority to order spending on it; it is said he spends without a judge's order because it apparently belongs to the foundling and he has guardianship to spend and purchase necessities like food and clothing because it is for spending. And similarly, others than the finder [spend] with the judge's order. His word is accepted regarding the amount of suitable maintenance. It is recommended to require the judge's permission if possible; otherwise, witnessing suffices.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rule from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: if property is found with the foundling, it belongs to him. The commentator explains that this is based on apparent circumstances (zahir): since the property is physically with the foundling, it is presumed to be his, and this presumption defends against claims of others. However, this is not an absolute proof of ownership but a legal presumption for practical purposes. The rule applies whether the money is tied to him or he is on an animal; the animal itself is also his. A historical anecdote from Baghdad illustrates the principle. The text distinguishes between property on his person (which is his) and property merely placed near him (which is lost property). Items on his bedding or under it are his, but buried under him is not. The commentator also discusses the procedure for spending on the foundling: ideally, the finder should get a judge's order, but if not possible, having witnesses suffices.

Key Terms
الظاهر (al-ẓāhir): The apparent circumstance or presumption used to establish a legal ruling in the absence of certainty.
لقطة (luqata): Lost and found property that must be announced and returned to its owner; if not claimed, it becomes a trust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a house in which a foundling is found alone?
A) It belongs to the foundling.
B) It is considered lost property.
C) It belongs to the finder.
D) There are two opinions.

Key Takeaway
Property found on the foundling's person or directly attached to him is his; property merely nearby is lost property (luqata).

The Finder's Limited Authority Over a Foundling: Marriage, Sale, and Lease
Details on limited guardianship of finder over foundling, with internal dispute on leasing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between acts the finder may and may not perform regarding a foundling, and state the relied-upon Hanafi position on leasing.

Core Idea
The finder's authority over a foundling is limited; he cannot marry, sell property, or lease for himself.

When someone finds and takes in a foundling, they become a guardian with restricted powers. They must provide care but cannot make major decisions without judicial oversight. This lesson clarifies what the finder may and may not do, focusing on marriage, sale, lease, and other actions, including an internal Hanafi dispute on leasing foundlings' services.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Start by noting that the finder (multaqit) lacks authority over marriage, sale, and lease of the foundling. Understand the reasons: no kinship, ownership, or sovereignty; for sale, the analogy to the mother and uncle shows the need for both complete judgment and compassion, which no single custodian possesses alone.
Marriage is absolutely prohibited because none of the three bases of guardianship exist.
2
Analyze the Dispute on Leasing
There are two Hanafi narrations: al-Quduri allows leasing; al-Jami' al-Saghir prohibits it. The text states the prohibition is preferred (asahh). Examine the reasoning: the prohibition is when the finder takes the wage for himself; if the wage is for the foundling, it is allowed.
The dispute is reconciled by distinguishing the beneficiary of the wage.
3
Examine Permitted Acts
The finder may hand the foundling over for a craft (hirafa) for training and preservation of property. He may also accept gifts on the foundling's behalf because it is pure benefit.
These are acts of necessity and benefit, not disposal for personal gain.
4
Identify Prohibited Acts Beyond the Core
Circumcision is not allowed; if performed and harm results, the finder is liable. The finder may move the foundling, but cannot move him from a city to a village or desert if detrimental.
These restrictions protect the foundling from harm.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence of the dispute on leasing: if a finder leases the foundling without a judge's order, the lease is invalid if the wage is for himself; it is valid if the wage is for the foundling.
The judge's permission may be required for leasing even for the foundling's benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا للملتقط عليه نكاح وبيع وإجارة أما النكاح فلانعدام سبب الولاية من القرابة والملك والسلطنة، وأما تصرفه في ماله بالبيع وغيره فبالقياس على الأم لأن ولاية التصرف لتثمير المال وذلك يتحقق بالرأي الكامل والشفقة الوافرة فلا بد من اجتماعهما والوجود في كل واحد منهما أحدهما، وأما الإجارة ففيها روايتان فرواية القدوري أنه يؤجره، وفي رواية الجامع الصغير أنه لا يجوز أن يؤجره ؛ كذا ذكره في الكراهية وهو الأصح. وجه الأول أنه يكون له وبه عرف أن الدار التي هو فيها وكذا البستان لا يكون له بالأولى. قوله : (وفي الجامع الصغير لا يجوز أن يؤجره) قال القهستاني في شرح النقاية : أي ليأخذ الأجرة لنفسه اعتباراً بالعم بخلاف الأم فإن لها إجارته .اهـ. وفي حاشية أبي السعود الذي يظهر حمل المنع من إجارته على ما إذا أجره الملتقط لتكون الأجرة لنفسه فلا ينافي ما ذكره القدوري لحمله على ما إذا كانت الأجرة للقيط، وما سبق عن القهستاني يشير إلى ذلك، وكذا تعليلهم المنع بإتلاف المنافع يشير إليه أيضاً فلا خلاف في الحقيقة .اهـ. فليتأمل وليراجع ما ذكره القهستاني . قوله : (ويسلمه في حرفة لأنه من باب تثقيفه وحفظ ماله والحرفة الصنعة. والتثقيف تقويم المعوج بالثقاف وهو ما يسوى به الرماح ويستعار للتأديب والتهذيب، كذا النهاية. قوله : ( ويقبض هبته لأنه نفع محض ولهذا يملكه الصغير بنفسه إذا كان عاقلاً وتملكه الأم ووصيها ولم يذكر ختانه . قال في الخانية : فليس له أن يختنه فإن فعل ذلك وهلك كان ضامناً اهـ . وفي الذخيرة : لو أمر الملتقط الختان فختنه ضمن الملتقط لأنه ليس له ولاية ختانه فصار بهذا الأمر جانياً ولا يضمن الختان. قيل : هذا إذا لم يعلم الختان بكونه ملتقطاً فإن علم ضمن اهـ. وقدمنا أنه له ولاية نقله إلى حيث شاء، وينبغي أن ليس له نقله من مصر إلى قرية أو بادية والله أعلم بالصواب.
His saying: (The finder does not have authority over [the foundling's] marriage, sale, or lease. As for marriage, because the cause of guardianship—kinship, ownership, or sovereignty—is absent. As for his disposal of property by sale etc., it is by analogy to the mother because the authority of disposal for the purpose of making the property productive requires both complete judgment and abundant compassion; these must be combined, and each of them [the mother and the paternal uncle] possesses only one. As for lease, there are two narrations: according to al-Quduri, he may lease him; according to a narration in al-Jami' al-Saghir, he may not lease him. This is mentioned in al-Karahiyya, and the latter is the more correct. The reasoning for the first is that it belongs to him, and by custom it is known that the house he lives in and likewise the garden do not belong to him by priority. His saying: (In al-Jami' al-Saghir, he may not lease him). Al-Quhistani said in Sharh al-Niqaya: i.e., to take the wage for himself, by analogy to the paternal uncle, unlike the mother who may lease him. In the gloss of Abu al-Su'ud, it appears that the prohibition of leasing is understood when the finder leases for his own benefit, so it does not contradict what al-Quduri mentioned, which is understood when the wage is for the foundling. What al-Quhistani said indicates this, and likewise their reasoning of prohibition by wasting benefits also indicates it, so there is no real disagreement. His saying: (He hands him over for a craft because it is part of his training and preservation of his property; craft is a trade. Tathqif is straightening a crooked thing with a thiqaf, which is used to straighten spears, and it is used metaphorically for discipline and refinement. This is from al-Nihaya. His saying: (He accepts a gift on his behalf) because it is pure benefit; for this reason, a minor may accept it himself if he is intelligent, and the mother and her executor may accept it. He did not mention circumcision. In al-Khaniyya: He may not circumcise him; if he does so and the foundling dies, he is liable. In al-Dhakhira: If the finder orders circumcision and the circumciser performs it, the finder is liable because he has no authority over circumcision; thus he becomes an aggressor by this order, and the circumciser is not liable. It is said: This is if the circumciser did not know the finder is a guardian; if he knew, he is liable. We have already stated that he has the authority to move him wherever he wishes, but it is not permissible to move him from a city to a village or desert.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the limitations of the finder's (multaqit's) guardianship over the foundling. The finder cannot marry off the foundling because he lacks any of the three bases for guardianship: kinship, ownership, or governmental authority. He cannot sell the foundling's property by analogy to the mother and paternal uncle: each has only one of the two necessary qualities (good judgment and compassion), so neither can act alone; rather, the judge must oversee. For leasing the foundling's services, there are two narrations: al-Quduri allows it, while al-Jami' al-Saghir prohibits it. The commentator explains that the prohibition is when the finder takes the wage for himself; if the wage is for the foundling, it is permitted. The finder may apprentice the foundling in a craft (hirafa) for training and preservation of property. He may accept gifts on the foundling's behalf because it is pure benefit. However, circumcision is not allowed; if the finder orders it and harm occurs, he is liable. The finder may move the foundling, but not from a city to a village or desert, as that would be detrimental.

Key Terms
الملتقط (al-multaqiṭ): The person who finds and takes custody of a foundling; serves as a guardian with limited authority.
الحرفة (al-ḥirfah): A craft or trade; the guardian may apprentice the foundling to learn a skill.
الختان (al-khitān): Circumcision; the guardian has no authority to perform it on the foundling without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (asahh) position in the passage, when is the finder (multaqit) not allowed to lease the foundling?
A) When the wage is for the foundling.
B) When the wage is for the finder himself.
C) When the lease is for a long term.
D) When the judge approves.

Key Takeaway
The finder's guardianship over a foundling is limited; leasing for personal gain is invalid, but apprenticeship and accepting gifts are permitted.

Definition and Initial Rulings of Lost Property (Luqatah)
Definition and initial rulings of luqatah from al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define luqatah technically according to the Hanafi school and identify its essential conditions (exposed to loss, unknown owner).

Core Idea
Luqatah is property exposed to loss with unknown owner — picking it up creates an amanah, not ownership.

This lesson opens the Book of Luqatah in al-Bahr al-Raiq. It clarifies the linguistic origin of the term, its technical definition, and the fundamental ruling that the finder holds it as a trust. You will learn to distinguish luqatah from similar categories like amanah and mubah property.

Guided Path
1
Parse the linguistic definitions
Understand why the term is pronounced with fatha on qaf as an intensive form, describing the property as something that attracts picking up.
Refer to the quotes from al-Qamus, al-Mughrib, and Fath al-Qadir in the passage.
2
Identify the technical definition
From al-Tatarkhaniyyah: property found with unknown owner and not mubah. Note what this excludes: known property and mubah property like wood in a forest.
3
Refine the definition with the condition of exposure to loss
The passage notes that property protected by a place or guardian is not luqatah, so the better definition adds 'exposed to loss' (ma'rid lil-daya').
This is the preferred Hanafi definition.
4
Apply the definition to the example
Analyze the drunkard case: garment on ground is luqatah (no liability), but under his head it is protected (liability).
The key is whether the property is protected by its owner.
5
Note the omission of the ruling on picking up
The author of al-Kanz omitted the ruling due to disagreement; the passage hints at ikhtilaf.
The ruling (mubah, mandub, or wajib) is addressed in later Hanafi texts.

كتاب اللقطة

لقطة الحل والحرم أمانة إن أخذها ليردها على ربها وأشهد وعرف إلى أن علم أن

كتاب اللقطة

.

وجه تأخيرها ظاهر . قال في القاموس : لقطه أخذه من الأرض فهو ملقوط واللقطة محركة كهمَزَة ما التقط .اهـ. وفي المغرب : اللقطة الشيء الذي تجده ملقى فتأخذه. قال الأزهري : ولم أسمع اللقطة بالسكون لغير الليث .اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : هي فعلة - بفتح العين - وصف مبالغة للفاعل كهمزة ولمزة ولعنة وضحكة للكثير الهمز وغيره. وبسكونها للمفعول كضحكة وهمزة للذي يضحك منه ويهزأ به وإنما قيل للمال لقطة - بالفتح - لأن طباع النفوس تتبادر إلى التقاطه لأنه مال فصار المال باعتبار أنه داع إلى أخذه لمعنى فيه نفسه كأنه الكثير الالتقاط مجازاً وإلا فحقيقته الملتقط الكثير الالتقاط. وما عن الأصمعي وابن الأعرابي أنه بفتح القاف اسم للمال أيضاً محمول على هذا يعني يطلق الالتقاط على المال أيضاً اهـ. ولم يذكر أكثر الشارحين تعريفها اصطلاحاً وعرفها في التتارخانية معزياً إلى المضمرات بأنها مال يوجد ولا يعرف له مالك وليس بمباح .اهـ. فخرج ما عرف مالكه فإنه أمانة لا لقطة ولأن حكمها التعريف وهذا لا يعرف بل يدفع إلى مالكه وخرج بالأخير مال الحربي لكن يرد عليه ما كان محرزاً بمكان أو حافظ فإنه ليس لقطة وهو داخل في التعريف فالأولى أن مال معرض للضياع. وعرفها في المحيط بأنها رفع شيء ضائع الغير لا للتمليك وجعل عدم الحافظ لها من شرائطها . ثم قال في آخر الباب: أخذ الثوب من السكران الواقع النائم على الأرض ليحفظه فهلك في يده لا ضمان عليه لأنه متاع ضائع كاللقطة، فإن كان الثوب تحت رأسه أو كانت دراهمه في كمه فأخذها ليحفظها فهو ضامن لأنه ليس بضائع لأنه محفوظ بمالكه .اهـ. والكلام فيها في مواضع في الالتقاط والملتقط واللقطة. أما الأول ولم يذكره المصنف للاختلاف فيه ففي الخلاصة : فإن خاف ضياعها

كتاب اللقطة

قوله : (لكن يرد عليه ما كان محرز الخ قال في النهر : الحرز بالمكان ونحوه خرج بقوله يوجد
Book of Luqatah. Luqatah in the state of [permissibility] and sanctity is a trust if one takes it intending to return it to its owner, and [he should] witness and announce until he knows [the owner returns]. The reason for its delay is obvious. He said in al-Qamus: 'laqatahu' means he picked it up from the ground, so it is 'malqut', and 'luqatah' (with a fatha on qaf) like 'humazah' means what is picked up. In al-Mughrib: 'luqatah' is the thing you find thrown away and take it. Al-Azhari said: I have not heard 'luqatah' with sukun except from al-Layth. In Fath al-Qadir: it is a 'fa'lah' pattern (with fatha on 'ayn) as an intensive description of the agent like 'humazah', 'lumazah', 'lu'nah', 'dahkah' for one who does much backbiting etc. And with sukun, it is for the object like 'dihkah' and 'humzah' for one who is laughed at and mocked. The property is called 'luqatah' (with fatha) because by nature souls hasten to pick it up since it is wealth; so the property, due to being a motivator for taking it for its own sake, is as if it is 'much-picking-up' metaphorically, otherwise its real meaning is the picker-up who picks up a lot. What al-Asma'i and Ibn al-A'rabi said that with fatha on qaf it is also a name for the property is carried on this, meaning that 'iltiqat' is also applied to the property. Most commentators did not mention its technical definition. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah, attributed to al-Mudmarat, it is defined as property that is found and whose owner is not known, and it is not permissible (mubah). So what is excluded is that whose owner is known; it is a trust, not luqatah, because its ruling is announcement, and this one is not known; rather it is returned to its owner. Also excluded by the last [condition] is the property of a harbi (enemy), but there is a problem: what is protected by a place or a guardian is not luqatah, yet it is included in the definition, so the better [definition] is property exposed to loss. In al-Muhit it is defined as picking up something that has been lost of another, not with the intention of ownership, and he made the absence of a guardian one of its conditions. Then he said at the end of the chapter: If one takes a garment from a drunken person lying sleeping on the ground to preserve it, and it perishes in his hand, there is no liability because it is lost property like luqatah. But if the garment is under his head or his dirhams are in his sleeve, and he takes them to preserve them, he is liable because it is not lost, as it is protected by its owner. The discussion involves three topics: the picking up, the finder, and the found property. As for the first, the author did not mention it due to disagreement. In al-Khulasa: if one fears its loss... (text breaks off).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Luqatah (lost property). It first gives the foundational ruling: picking up luqatah is an amanah (trust) if done with the intention to return it to its owner; the finder should announce it and wait until the owner is identified. The author then explores linguistic definitions from al-Qamus, al-Mughrib, and Fath al-Qadir, explaining that 'luqatah' with a fatha on qaf is an intensive form describing the property as something that attracts picking up. The technical definition is taken from al-Tatarkhaniyyah: property found whose owner is unknown and which is not mubah (freely permissible). This excludes known property (it is an amanah, not luqatah) and war booty. However, property that is secured in a place or with a guardian is not considered luqatah, so the better definition is 'property exposed to loss' (ma'rid lil-daya'). The passage also gives an example: taking a garment from a sleeping drunkard on the ground is like luqatah (no liability), but if it's under his head, it's protected so liability attaches. Finally, it notes that the chapter will cover three issues: picking up, the finder, and the found property, but the author (al-Kanz) omitted the first due to disagreement.

Key Terms
لقطة (luqaṭah): Property found that has no known owner and is exposed to loss; the finder takes it as a trust to return.
التقاط (iltiqāṭ): The act of picking up lost property; also used metaphorically for the property itself.
مال ضائع (māl ḍā'i'): Property that is lost and not under any protection (hifz).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition from al-Tatarkhaniyyah mentioned in the passage, which of the following would be considered luqatah?
A) A wallet whose owner is known.
B) A piece of wood found in a forest with no owner.
C) A bag of money found in the desert with no owner and no protection.
D) A horse belonging to an enemy soldier captured in war.

Key Takeaway
Luqatah is not found property you can keep; it is a trust (amanah) with obligations of announcement and return.

Defining Luqatah: Linguistic Roots and Legal Conditions
Defining luqatah linguistically and terminologically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of luqatah, identify its linguistic basis, and apply the condition of non-securement to distinguish luqatah from amanah.

Core Idea
Luqatah is property exposed to loss without a keeper—taking it makes you a trustee, not an owner.

In this lesson, we explore the precise legal definition of luqatah (lost property) as established by Hanafi jurists. The passage from Bahr al-Raiq examines both linguistic and terminological definitions, refining them to exclude items that are secured or have known owners. You will learn the key conditions that turn found property into luqatah and the legal consequences for the finder.

Guided Path
1
Trace the linguistic derivation
Start with the root 'ل ق ط' meaning 'to pick up'. The form 'لُقَطَة' (with fatha) is a hyperbolic noun for the picker, but metaphorically used for the picked property. Understand this shift from agent to object.
The passage cites al-Qamus, al-Maghrib, and Fath al-Qadir to establish the linguistic basis.
2
Extract the core terminological definition
From al-Tatar Khaniyya: 'property found whose owner is unknown and is not mubah'. Identify each component and its legal significance.
This definition excludes items that are mubah (like water) or have known owners.
3
Identify the refinement from al-Muhit
Al-Muhit adds 'absence of a keeper' as a condition. Note why secured items are not luqatah even if owner unknown.
The author suggests 'property exposed to loss' is more accurate to exclude secured items.
4
Apply the definition to the edge case
Analyze the example of a cloth from a drunken sleeper: if on the ground, it is luqatah (lost); if under his head, it is secured and not lost. See how the condition of 'not secured' is applied.
The ruling changes: no liability for lost item, but liability for secured item.
5
State the legal consequence
If the finder takes with intent to return and announces, the luqatah becomes a trust (amanah). Liability only arises if the finder is negligent or if the item was secured.
This aligns with the opening: 'luqatah of halal and haram is a trust'.

Source Text

Book of Luqatah. Luqatah of halal and haram is a trust if one takes it to return it to its owner and witnesses and announces until he knows that... The reason for its delay is apparent. He said in al-Qamus: 'Laqatahu' means he took it from the ground, so it is 'malqut', and 'luqatah' (with vowel movement) like 'humazah' means what is picked up. End. And in al-Maghrib: 'Luqatah' is the thing you find thrown and take it. al-Azhari said: I have not heard 'luqatah' with sukun except from al-Layth. End. And in Fath al-Qadir: It is a 'fi'lah' (with fatha on the 'ayn) as a hyperbolic description of the subject like 'humazah', 'lumazah', 'la'nah', 'dahkah' for one who does much of something. With sukun it is for the object like 'duhkah' and 'huz'ah' for one who is laughed at and mocked. Rather the property is called 'luqatah' (with fatha) because the nature of souls hastens to pick it up because it is wealth, so the wealth is considered, as it invites its taking due to a meaning in itself, as if it is 'much picking up' figuratively; otherwise its reality is the one who picks up much. What is reported from al-Asma'i and Ibn al-A'rabi that with fatha of qaf it is also a name for the property is interpreted in this way, meaning that 'iltiqat' (picking up) is also applied to wealth. End. Most commentators did not mention a terminological definition. In al-Tatar Khaniyya, attributed to al-Mudmarat, it is defined as property that is found whose owner is not known and is not permissible. End. Thus excluded is that whose owner is known, for it is a trust, not luqatah, because its ruling is announcement, but this one is not known, so it is given to its owner. Also excluded by the last is the property of a harbi. But there is a problem: what is secured in a place or with a keeper is not luqatah, while it is included in the definition. So better is: property exposed to loss. It is defined in al-Muhit as: taking something lost of another, not for ownership, and making the absence of a keeper a condition. Then he said at the end of the chapter: If one takes a cloth from an intoxicated person who fell and slept on the ground to preserve it, and it perishes in his hand, there is no liability because it is lost property like luqatah. If the cloth was under his head or his dirhams were in his sleeve and he took them to preserve them, he is liable because it is not lost, being preserved by its owner. End. Meaning: on the ground as lost, because that is not said of that which is secured, as in al-Muhit it made non-securement a condition.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed definition of luqatah (lost property). It begins with linguistic definitions from classical lexicons: al-Qamus, al-Maghrib, and Fath al-Qadir explaining that 'luqatah' with a fatha is a hyperbolic form for the one who picks up, but is used metaphorically for the property itself. The terminological definition from al-Tatar Khaniyya states it is property found whose owner is unknown and is not mubah (unrestricted permissible). This excludes known property (then it is an amanah) and property of a harbi. A problem is noted: items secured in a place or with a keeper fall under the definition but are not considered lost, so the author suggests 'property exposed to loss' is more accurate. The definition from al-Muhit adds the condition that there be no keeper, and gives an example: taking a cloth from a drunken sleeper on the ground—if lost, no liability because it is like luqatah; but if it was under his head (secured), then the taker is liable. The marginal note emphasizes the condition of being on the ground as lost.

Key Terms
لقطة (luqatah): Property found whose owner is unknown, not mubah, exposed to loss, and with no keeper.
ملتقط (multaqit): The person who picks up the luqatah.
أمانة (amanah): A trust; luqatah becomes an amanah when the finder takes it with intention to return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The owner's identity must be unknown
B) The item must not be secured by a keeper or place
C) The finder must be an adult
D) The item must be permissible

Key Takeaway
The essential Hanafi definition of luqatah is property found whose owner is unknown, not mubah, and not secured—making the finder a trustee, not an owner.

Ruling on Picking Up a Lost Item: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explaining the ruling categories for picking up lost items and refuting Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on picking up a lost item, explain the Shafi'i counterargument and the Hanafi rebuttal, and identify the mufta bihi position among variant Hanafi views.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds that picking up a lost item is recommended, not obligatory, even when fearing loss.

When you find a lost item, are you required to pick it up? The schools differ. The Shafi'is argue that leaving a lost item that might be lost is waste (tadhyi'), which is haram, making picking it up obligatory. The Hanafis respond that leaving is not waste because one is not obligated to preserve what one has not undertaken to protect. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of the ikhtilaf and clarifies the operative ruling in the Hanafi madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its possible rulings
The act is picking up a lost item (al-iltiqat). Scholars have classified it under different ahkam: obligatory, recommended, permissible, forbidden. Identify which ruling is at the center of the dispute.
Focus on the case where the finder fears the item will be lost.
2
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that if one fears loss, picking up is obligatory. His proof: leaving would cause waste (tadhyi'), and waste is prohibited (haram). Thus what prevents waste is obligatory.
The Shafi'i argument is based on the principle that preserving property is required.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: leaving is not waste. It is merely refraining from an uncommitted act of preservation, similar to refusing a deposit (al-wadi'ah). Since there is no prior obligation to preserve, there is no waste.
The Hanafi argument distinguishes between active destruction and passive non-intervention.
4
Navigate the intra-Hanafi differences
Some Hanafi texts (e.g., al-Khulasah) state that picking up is obligatory when fearing loss. Others (al-Bada'i', al-Muhit) say it is mandub. The later authorities clarify that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is mandub, not wajib.
The view in al-Khulasah is considered shadhdh; the madhhab is that of al-Muhit and others.
5
Apply the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Shafi'i, leaving when fearing loss is a sin. Under Hanafi, it is not sinful but may be suboptimal. In practice, the Hanafi mufta bihi recommends picking up if one trusts oneself to return it; otherwise leaving is better.
The case from Jami' al-Fusulayn supports non-liability for non-taking, reinforcing the non-obligation stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام فيها في مواضع في الالتقاط والملتقط واللقطة. أما الأول ولم يذكره المصنف للاختلاف فيه ففي الخلاصة : فإن خاف ضياعها يقال هي معصوم للحفظ على يفترض الرفع، وإن لم يخف يباح رفعها ؛ أجمع العلماء عليه والأفضل الرفع في ظاهر المذهب اهـ. وأقره عليه في فتح القدير وفي البدائع أنه مندوب الأخذ ومباحه وحرامه. فالأول أن يخاف عليها الضياع لو تركها لأنه إحياء لمال المسلم فكان مستحباً. وقال الشافعي : إذا خاف الضياع وجب أخذها وإلا استحب لأن الترك عند الخوف تضييع والتضييع حرام وهذا غير سديد، لأن الترك لا يكون تضييعاً بل امتناع عن حفظ غير ملتزم وهو ليس بتضييع كالامتناع عن قبول الوديعة. وأما حالة الإباحة فأن لا يخاف الضياع وأما حالة الحرمة فهو أن يأخذها لنفسه لا لصاحبها فتكون في معنى الغصب .اهـ. فقد علمت أن ما في الخلاصة ليس مذهبنا . وفي المحيط أن الأخذ مندوب إن أمن على نفسه التعريف والرد على صاحبها. وإن خاف الضياع فعليه أن يأخذها صيانة لحق المسلم لأن لماله حرمة كما لنفسه، وإن كان لا يأمن على نفسه فالترك أولى اهـ . وهو موافق لما في الخلاصة ومثله في المجتبى. وأشار في الهداية إلى التبري منه بقوله وهو واجب إذا خاف الضياع على ما قالوا ولم أر حكم ما إذا ضاعت بعدما خاف الضياع ولم يلتقطها، ومقتضى القول بافتراض رفعها الضمان لو لم يرفع وضاعت لكن في جامع الفصولين في الفصل الثالث والثلاثين : لو انفتح زق فمر به رجل فلو لم يأخذه برئ ولو أخذه ثم تركه ضمن لو مالكه غائباً لا لو حاضراً، وكذا لو رأى ما وقع من كم رجل اهـ. فهذا يدل على عدم الافتراض إلا أن يقال : إن فائدة الافتراض الإثم بالترك لا الضمان في الدنيا بدليل أنهم قالوا لو منع المالك عن أمواله حتى هلكت يأثم ولا يضمن. قوله : (فقد علمت أن ما في الخلاصة ليس مذهبنا قال في النهر : ما في البدائع شاذ وما في الخلاصة جرى عليه في المحيط والتتارخانية والاختيار وارتضاه في الفتح وقيده في السراجية بأن يأمن على نفسه ردها. قوله : (ولم أر حكم ما إذا ضاعت بعد ما خاف الضياع الخ) أقول : ذكر في الخانية ما هو كالصريح في عدم ضمانه في الصورة المذكورة حيث قال رجل التقط لقطة ليعرفها ثم أعادها إلى المكان الذي وجدها فيه ذكر في الكتاب أنه يبرأ عن الضمان ولم يفصل بين ما إذا تحول ذلك المكان ثم أعادها إليه وبين ما إذا أعادها قبل أن يتحول قال أبو جعفر : إنما يبرأ إذا عن
The discussion of it is in places: regarding picking up, the finder, and the found item. As for the first, the author did not mention it due to difference of opinion. In al-Khulasah: If he fears its loss, it is said: it is protected, so picking it up is obligatory; if he does not fear, picking it up is permissible; scholars agree on it, and the best is to pick up according to the apparent madhhab. End. And it is confirmed in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Bada'i', picking up is recommended, permissible, and forbidden. The first: if he fears loss if left, because it is reviving a Muslim's property, so it is recommended. Al-Shafi'i said: If he fears loss, it is obligatory to take it; otherwise, it is recommended, because leaving when fearing loss is waste, and waste is haram. This is not correct, because leaving is not waste; it is refraining from an uncommitted preservation, and it is not waste like refraining from accepting a deposit. As for the permissible case: when he does not fear loss. As for the forbidden case: when he takes it for himself, not for its owner, so it is like usurpation. End. So you know that what is in al-Khulasah is not our madhhab. In al-Muhit: Taking is recommended if he trusts himself to announce and return to its owner. If he fears loss, he must take it to safeguard the right of a Muslim, because his property has sanctity like his soul; if he does not trust himself, then leaving is better. End. And it agrees with al-Khulasah, and similar is in al-Mujtaba. Al-Hidayah indicated disassociation from it by saying: It is obligatory when fearing loss according to what they said, but I have not seen the ruling if it is lost after he feared loss and did not pick it up. The requirement of the view of obligatory picking up is liability if he did not pick up and it was lost. However, in Jami' al-Fusulayn, chapter 33: If a wineskin bursts and a man passes by, if he does not take it he is innocent, but if he takes it then leaves it, he is liable if the owner is absent, not if present. Similarly, if he sees something fall from someone's sleeve. End. This indicates that it is not obligatory. Unless it is said: The benefit of obligation is sin for leaving, not liability in this world, as they said: If the owner prevents his wealth until it perishes, he sins but does not owe indemnity. The saying: 'So you know that what is in al-Khulasah is not our madhhab' – in al-Nahr: What is in al-Bada'i' is anomalous, and what is in al-Khulasah is followed by al-Muhit, al-Tatar Khaniyya, al-Ikhtiyar, and is approved by al-Fath, and restricted in al-Sirajiyya by the condition that he trusts himself to return it. The saying: 'I have not seen the ruling if it is lost after he feared loss' – I say: In al-Khaniyya it is almost explicit about no liability in that case, where it says: A man picked up a luqatah to announce it, then returned it to the place he found it; it is mentioned in the book that he is free from liability without distinguishing whether the place had changed or not. Abu Ja'far said: He is free only if...
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling (hukm) of picking up a lost item. The author notes that the original text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) omitted this due to ikhtilaf. Several views are cited: In al-Khulasah, it is said that if one fears loss, picking up is obligatory (فعل رفع)، otherwise permissible. In al-Bada'i', it is divided into mandub (recommended), mubah (permissible), and haram (forbidden). The mandub case is when one fears loss, as it revives a Muslim's property. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that if one fears loss, it becomes obligatory, because leaving would be tadhyi' (waste) which is haram. The Hanafi rebuttal is that leaving is not waste; it is simply refraining from an uncommitted preservation, like refusing a deposit. The author then clarifies that the view in al-Khulasah is not the relied-upon madhhab; the mufta bihi is that it is mandub when one trusts to announce and return, and if one fears loss, one must take it (some say wajib, but the preferred view is mandub). A case from Jami' al-Fusulayn is given: if a wineskin bursts and a passerby does not take it, he is not liable; if he takes it and later leaves it, he is liable only if the owner is absent. This supports non-obligation. The marginal notes elaborate that the view of obligation is anomalous, and the correct view is that of al-Muhit and others.

Key Terms
الالتقاط (al-iltiqat): The act of lifting a lost item from the ground to identify and return it.
تضييع (tadhyi'): Waste or destruction; Shafi'i argues leaving lost property when fearing loss constitutes tadhyi'.
مندوب (mandub): Recommended act; rewarded if done, not sinful if left.
خلاف (khilaf): Scholarly disagreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i argument that leaving a lost item when fearing loss is tadhyi'?
A) It is waste because it leads to loss.
B) It is not waste because one is not obligated to preserve it.
C) It is waste only if the finder is present.
D) It is waste regardless.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi for picking up a lost item is mandub when the finder trusts to return it; leaving when fearing loss is not a sin, contrary to the Shafi'i position.

Luqatah — Found Property

Conditions of the Finder (Multaqit) in Hanafi Fiqh
Discussing whether the finder must be adult and free

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the conditions of the finder (multaqit) in luqatah and identify the evidence from al-Mujtaba and al-Bazzaziyya.

Core Idea
Neither adulthood nor freedom is a condition for a valid finder of luqatah.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling on who can validly pick up lost property (luqatah). While many assume only adult free Muslims may act, the Hanafi school permits minors and slaves. We examine the textual evidence from al-Mujtaba and al-Bazzaziyya that supports this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed conditions
The passage questions whether majority (bulugh) and freedom (hurriyya) are required for the finder. Determine the author's stance and note the two conditions examined.
2
Analyze the proof for majority not being a condition
From al-Mujtaba: the announcement (ta'rif) is made to the guardian of the minor and the heir. This indicates that a minor's picking up is valid, as someone acts on his behalf.
The proof is indirect: the guardian's role confirms the minor's act is not void.
3
Analyze the proof for freedom not being a condition
From al-Bazzaziyya: a master cannot take a deposit from his slave's hand without inspection, because the slave has a valid hand (yad sahiha). Analogously, a slave can validly hold luqatah.
The slave's possession is legally recognized even if restricted (mahjur).
4
Examine the analogy with foundling (laqit)
The author links luqatah to laqit: if a slave picks up a foundling, the ruling depends on whether the slave is mahjur (restricted) or ma'dhun (permitted). This suggests a similar distinction for luqatah.
The author notes unresolved issues about announcement and ownership for slaves.
5
Note the unresolved issues
The passage leaves open questions: who handles the announcement for a slave? And does a slave become owner after announcement? These are left for further study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الملتقط فلم أر من بين شرائطه ولا يشترط بلوغه بدليل ما في المجتبى التعريف إلى ولي الصبي والوارث اهـ. فدل على صحة التقاطه. وأما حرية الملتقط فليست بشرط لأن للعبد يداً صحيحة بدليل قولهم كما في البزازية من الوديعة ليس للمالك أن يأخذ وديعة عبده مأذوناً أم لا ما لم يحضر ويظهر أنه من كسبه لاحتمال أن تكون وديعة الغير في يد العبد، فإن للعبد تدفع إليه اهـ . لكن قدمنا أنه لو التقط لقيطاً فقال المولى هو عبدي وقال العبد التقطته فإن محجوراً فالقول للمولى و إن مأذوناً فللعبد ولم أر حكم اللقطة إذا تنازعا فيها وينبغي أن يكون كذلك، ولم أر حكم تعريف لقطته هل إليه أو إلى مولاه، وإذا عرفت فهل يتملكها
As for the finder, I have not seen among its conditions that he must be adult; majority is not a condition, as evidenced by what is in al-Mujtaba: the announcement is made to the guardian of the minor and the heir. End. This indicates the validity of his picking up. As for the freedom of the finder, it is not a condition because the slave has a valid hand (possession), as evidenced by their saying, as in al-Bazzaziyya about deposits: the owner cannot take the deposit of his slave, whether the slave is permitted or not, until he inspects and it appears to be from his earnings because it could be a deposit of someone else in the slave's hand. The slave is allowed to pay it. End. But we previously said: If he picks up a foundling (laqit), and the master says 'he is my slave' and the slave says 'I picked him up', then if the slave is ma'jur (restricted), the word of the master; if mi'dhun (permitted), the word of the slave. I have not seen the ruling for luqatah if they dispute, and it should be similar. I have not seen the ruling for his announcement of luqatah: is it up to him or to his master? And if he announces, does he become the owner?
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage addresses the conditions of the finder (الملتقط). The author states that majority (بلوغ) is not a condition for validity; he cites al-Mujtaba that the guardian of a minor or the heir can do the announcement on his behalf, proving the child's action is valid. Similarly, freedom is not a condition because a slave has a valid hand (يد صحيحة) for possession, as shown by the rule from al-Bazzaziyya that a master cannot simply take a deposit from his slave's hand without inspection, as it may belong to another. The slave has the right to pay it. Then a comparison is made with foundling (لقيط): if a slave picks up a foundling and there is a dispute with the master, the ruling depends on whether the slave is restricted (mahjur) or permitted (ma'dhun). The author notes he hasn't seen a specific ruling for luqatah but analogizes it. He also notes uncertainty about who handles the announcement and whether the finder gains ownership. The overall point is that legal capacity for picking up is not restricted to free adult males.

Key Terms
يد صحيحة (yad sahiha): Legally valid possession; the slave's hand is considered valid for holding property.
محجور (mahjur): A slave restricted from financial transactions; his master has control.
ملتقط (multaqit): The finder who picks up lost property (luqatah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because a slave is not required to pray.
B) Because a slave has a valid hand for possession.
C) Because the master is responsible.
D) Because the luqatah belongs to the master.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a minor or slave can validly pick up luqatah, though announcement and ownership are subject to their legal status.

Luqatah — Found Property

Ruling on Picking Up Lost Property and the Shafi'i Dispute
States the ruling on picking up lost property and refutes Shafi'i view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on picking up lost property in three scenarios and explain why the Hanafi school rejects the Shafi'i view that it becomes obligatory.

Core Idea
Picking up lost property is recommended, not obligatory — abandonment without a duty to preserve is not waste.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the ruling on picking up lost property (luqata). It clarifies that the Hanafi position distinguishes three scenarios based on intent and risk of loss. The passage then refutes the Shafi'i claim that picking up becomes obligatory when there is fear of loss, providing a counter-analogy from the law of trusts (wadi'ah). Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the Hanafi principle that an act does not become wajib merely to avoid a hypothetical loss where no duty exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and list the three conditions for picking up lost property: fear of loss, no fear of loss, and taking for oneself.
Each scenario has a different hukm: mandub, mubah, and haram respectively.
2
State the Hanafi position on the fear-of-loss scenario
Note that the Hanafi school says it is mandub (recommended), not wajib, even when the property might be lost.
The text uses 'مستحب' and 'مندوب' interchangeably.
3
Identify the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'i school argues that leaving it at a time of fear is waste (تضييع), which is haram, so picking up becomes obligatory to avoid haram.
This is the core point of dispute.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response compares leaving the property to refusing a trust: it is abstention from an unrequired responsibility, not active waste.
The analogy (qiyas) to wadi'ah is crucial.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a Hanafi who leaves the item does not sin, while a Shafi'i would sin; conversely, taking it is rewarded only in Hanafi, obligatory in Shafi'i.
This affects a person's religious duty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفترض الرفع، وإن لم يخف يباح رفعها ؛ أجمع العلماء عليه والأفضل الرفع في ظاهر المذهب اهـ. وأقره عليه في فتح القدير وفي البدائع أنه مندوب الأخذ ومباحه وحرامه. فالأول أن يخاف عليها الضياع لو تركها لأنه إحياء لمال المسلم فكان مستحباً. وقال الشافعي : إذا خاف الضياع وجب أخذها وإلا استحب لأن الترك عند الخوف تضييع والتضييع حرام وهذا غير سديد، لأن الترك لا يكون تضييعاً بل امتناع عن حفظ غير ملتزم وهو ليس بتضييع كالامتناع عن قبول الوديعة. وأما حالة الإباحة فأن لا يخاف الضياع وأما حالة الحرمة فهو أن يأخذها لنفسه لا لصاحبها فتكون في معنى الغصب .اهـ.
It is assumed that picking up is obligatory, and if one does not fear [loss], it is permissible to pick it up; the scholars agreed upon it, and the superior [view] is picking up in the apparent madhhab. In Fath al-Qadeer it is affirmed upon it, and in al-Bada'i' that it is recommended, permissible, and forbidden. The first is that he fears loss if he leaves it, because it is reviving the property of a Muslim, so it is recommended. Al-Shafi'i said: If he fears loss, picking it up is obligatory, otherwise recommended, because leaving it at time of fear is wasting and wasting is prohibited. But this is unsound, because leaving is not wasting, but rather abstention from uncommitted preservation, and it is not wasting, like abstention from accepting a trust. As for the permissible state, it is that he does not fear loss. As for the forbidden state, it is that he takes it for himself not for its owner, thus it becomes like usurpation.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the ruling of picking up lost property (لقطة). The author states that there is a presumption that picking up is obligatory (يفترض الرفع), but then clarifies that if there is no fear of loss, it is merely permissible (مباح). He notes that the preferred view in the apparent madhhab is that picking up is better (الأفضل). Then he elaborates on the three categories mentioned in al-Bada'i': recommended (مندوب) when there is fear of loss because it is like reviving a Muslim's property; permissible (مباح) when no fear; and forbidden (حرام) when taken for oneself intending not to return to the owner, which is akin to usurpation. The passage then presents the view of Imam al-Shafi'i, who makes it obligatory when there is fear of loss, arguing that leaving it would be a waste which is Haram. The Hanafi response is that leaving it is not waste; it is simply refraining from taking on an unrequired responsibility, similar to refusing to accept a trust (وديعة). Thus, the Hanafi view maintains that picking up is not obligatory but recommended in that scenario.

Key Terms
اللقطة (al-luqata): Lost property found by someone who takes it with intent to preserve and advertise for the owner.
التقاط (al-iltiqat): The act of picking up lost property.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): A trust or deposit, used here in analogy to show that leaving is not waste.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab as stated in this passage, which of the following best describes the ruling on picking up lost property when there is no fear of loss?
A) Obligatory (wajib)
B) Recommended (mandub)
C) Permissible (mubah)
D) Forbidden (haram)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on lost property is context-dependent: recommended when fear of loss, permissible when safe, and forbidden if taken for oneself.

Luqatah — Found Property
intermediate
8 min
Conditions of the Finder of Lost Property
Expansion on the conditions of the finder with proofs from Hanafi sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the required qualifications for a finder of lost property and identify the dalil used to support the validity of a child, slave, or non-Muslim as a finder.

Core Idea
No qualification beyond a valid hand: a child, slave, or non-Muslim may validly pick up lost property.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that the finder of lost property (al-multaqit) does not need to be an adult, free, or Muslim. The source from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the base text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, citing evidence from earlier Hanafi works to justify the validity of picking up by those who lack these attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions being examined
The author considers three potential conditions: adulthood (بلوغ), freedom (حرية), and Islam (إسلام). He argues that none are required.
2
Examine the dalil for each condition
For adulthood: the ruling in al-Mujtaba that announcement is made to the child's guardian proves the child's picking up is valid. For freedom: the slave's valid hand is shown by the ruling that the owner cannot take a trust from his slave. For non-Muslim: the acceptance of disbelieving witnesses against a disbelieving finder proves validity.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
How does each piece of evidence directly support the ruling? E.g., the child's announcement being handled by guardian implies the child's initial act is valid; the slave's valid hand in trusts implies validity in picking up; witness acceptance implies the finder's act is recognized.
4
Note unresolved issues and implications
The author leaves several questions unanswered: disputes between master and slave, the mukatib's status, and the apostate's picking up. This shows the development of fiqh through exploration.
5
State the Hanafi mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view is that any person with a valid hand can pick up lost property; conditions of adulthood, freedom, and Islam are not required. The duties of announcement etc. apply equally.

Source Text
