Scattered Date Pits and Peels: Ruling and Condition
Ruling on scattered waste items and condition of being collected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on taking scattered date pits and pomegranate peels and explain the condition that they be scattered.

Core Idea
Scattered date pits and peels are mubah to take without announcement, but remain the owner's property.

The Hanafi school recognizes that some items are by custom not sought by their owners. The classical example is date pits and pomegranate peels. This lesson clarifies the ruling and the important condition that the items be scattered, as opposed to collected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items
State the specific examples given in the passage: date pits and pomegranate peels.
These are items people typically discard as waste.
2
State the default ruling
The item is mubah (permissible) to take and use without any announcement (ta'rif).
The owner is known not to seek them.
3
Apply the critical condition
This ruling only applies when the items are found scattered (mutafarriqa), not collected in a pile.
Al-Bazzaziyya restricts the absolute statement of al-Hidayah.
4
Understand ownership
Even though taking is permissible, the item remains the property of the original owner. If the owner appears, they can reclaim it.
Transferring ownership from an unknown person is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا صح أن التعريف وأطلق المصنف في تعريفها وهو مقيد بما في الهداية فإن كانت اللقطة شيئاً يعلم صاحبها لا يطلبها كالنواة وقشر الرمان يكون إلقاؤه إباحة حتى جاز الانتفاع به من غير تعريف ولكنه يبقى على ملك مالكه لأن التمليك من المجهول لا يصح . وفي البزازية : لو وجدها مالكها في يده له أخذها إلا إذا قال عند الرمي من أخذها فهي له لقوم معلومين، ولم يذكر السرخسي هذا التفصيل وكذا الحكم في التقاط السنابل لكن أخذه بعد جمع غيره يعد دناءة. وأطلق في الهداية في النواة وقشور الرمان وقيده في البزازية بأن يكون في مواضع متفرقة قال : أما المجتمعة فهي من قبيل ما يطلبه صاحبه فيحفظه وإن وجد جوزة ثم وثم حتى بلغ المتقوم إن مجتمعاً فهو من الثاني، وإن متفرقاً له قيمة اختلفوا؛ قيل من الأول، وقيل من الثاني وهو الأحوط . وذكر في الفتاوى المختار أنه من النوع الأول التفاح والكمثرى إن وجد في الماء يجوز أخذه وإن كثيراً لأنه يفسد بالماء والحطب في الماء إن لم يكن له قيمة يأخذه وإن له قيمة فهو لقطة . وجعل في الفتاوى الحطب كالتفاح بالماء، أصابوا بعيراً مذبوحاً في البادية قريباً الماء من ووقع في ظنه أن مالكه أباحه لا بأس بالأخذ والأكل. وعن الثاني لو طرح ميتة فجاء آخر وأخذ صوفها له الانتفاع به ولو جاء مالكها له أن يأخذ الصوف منه، ولو سلخها ودبغ الجلد يأخذه المالك ويرد عليه ما زاد الدباغ فيه اهـ.
If it is correct that announcement (is required), and the author made an absolute statement about it, but it is qualified by what is in al-Hidayah: if the found item is something that the owner is known not to seek, like a date pit and pomegranate peel, then discarding it is permissible, so it is lawful to benefit from it without announcement, but it remains the property of its owner because transferring ownership from an unknown person is not valid. In al-Bazzaziyya: If the owner finds it in his hand, he may take it back, unless he said at the time of discarding: 'Whoever takes it, it is his' to known people. Al-Sarakhsi did not mention this detail. Similarly, the ruling for picking up ears of grain, but taking them after another's collection is considered lowly. In al-Hidayah, he made an absolute statement regarding date pits and pomegranate peels, but in al-Bazzaziyya he restricted it to scattered locations. He said: As for collected ones, they are among what the owner seeks, so he preserves them. If he finds a nut and then another, until it reaches a valuable amount: if collected, it is of the second type (requires preservation); if scattered with value, they disagreed: some say it is the first type, some say the second, and that is more cautious. In al-Fatawa al-Mukhtar, apples and pears found in water are of the first type; it is permissible to take them even if many because they spoil in water. Likewise, firewood in water: if it has no value, he takes it; if it has value, it is a lost item. In al-Fatawa, firewood is treated like apples in water. If they find a slaughtered camel near water in the desert and it is probable that its owner allowed it, there is no harm in taking and eating it. As for another case: if a dead animal is thrown, and another comes and takes its wool, he may benefit from it. But if its owner comes, he may take the wool from him. If he skinned it and tanned the hide, the owner takes it and returns the increase due to tanning. End quote.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage discusses items that are presumed to be abandoned by their owner, thus not requiring formal announcement. The core examples are date pits and pomegranate peels, which people typically discard as waste. Such items are considered mubah (permissible) to take without announcement, though they remain the property of the original owner until explicitly given away. If the owner appears, he can reclaim them. The text qualifies that this applies only when the pits and peels are found scattered; if they are collected (e.g., in a pile), they are considered to be owned and thus treated as regular lost items. Similarly, nuts and other items: if scattered individually, they are like date pits; if collected in a pile, they require preservation. Apples and pears in water are permissible to take because they spoil quickly. Firewood in water: valueless firewood can be taken; valuable firewood is a lost item. A slaughtered camel near water in the desert, if the finder thinks the owner allowed it, can be taken and eaten. A dead animal's wool: a finder may take and use the wool, but the owner can reclaim it; if the hide is tanned, the owner gets the hide back but must compensate for the tanning. All these rulings illustrate the principle that items clearly abandoned or perishable do not require the full procedure of ta'rif.

Key Terms
إباحة (ibaha): Permissibility to take and use without liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazzaziyya, when is a found date pit considered mubah without announcement?
A) Whenever it is found anywhere
B) Only if it is found scattered
C) Only if it is found in a pile
D) Only if the owner explicitly gives permission

Key Takeaway
Scattered date pits and pomegranate peels are mubah to take, but the finder does not own them; the owner can reclaim them.

Special Cases: Water Items, Desert Camel, and Wool/Hide
Ruling on specific cases: water items, desert camel, and wool/hide.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of presumed abandonment to items in water, a slaughtered camel in the desert, and wool from a dead animal.

Core Idea
Perishable items in water and items in desert where owner likely allowed use are also mubah.

The Hanafi school extends the principle of presumed abandonment beyond date pits to other scenarios where the owner's intent is clear. This lesson covers apples and firewood in water, a slaughtered camel near water, and wool from a dead animal. Each case illustrates how perishability and custom determine the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify each scenario
List the examples: apples and pears in water, firewood in water, a slaughtered camel near water, and wool/hide from a dead animal.
Each has a different nuance.
2
Determine the common principle
In each case, the owner is either known not to seek the item (perishable) or is likely to have allowed its use (desert camel).
The custom of abandonment is key.
3
Apply the value condition
For firewood in water: if worthless, mubah; if valuable, it is luqatah. Similarly, apples in water are mubah because they spoil.
Value determines whether the owner would bother to retrieve.
4
Understand the owner's reclaim right
Even if taking is mubah, the owner can reclaim the item. For tanned hide, the owner must compensate for the increase due to tanning.
Ownership remains unless explicitly given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If it is correct that announcement (is required), and the author made an absolute statement about it, but it is qualified by what is in al-Hidayah: if the found item is something that the owner is known not to seek, like a date pit and pomegranate peel, then discarding it is permissible, so it is lawful to benefit from it without announcement, but it remains the property of its owner because transferring ownership from an unknown person is not valid. Commentary
This passage discusses items that are presumed to be abandoned by their owner, thus not requiring formal announcement. The core examples are date pits and pomegranate peels, which people typically discard as waste. Such items are considered mubah (permissible) to take without announcement, though they remain the property of the original owner until explicitly given away. If the owner appears, he can reclaim them. The text qualifies that this applies only when the pits and peels are found scattered; if they are collected (e.g., in a pile), they are considered to be owned and thus treated as regular lost items. Similarly, nuts and other items: if scattered individually, they are like date pits; if collected in a pile, they require preservation. Apples and pears in water are permissible to take because they spoil quickly. Firewood in water: valueless firewood can be taken; valuable firewood is a lost item. A slaughtered camel near water in the desert, if the finder thinks the owner allowed it, can be taken and eaten. A dead animal's wool: a finder may take and use the wool, but the owner can reclaim it; if the hide is tanned, the owner gets the hide back but must compensate for the tanning. All these rulings illustrate the principle that items clearly abandoned or perishable do not require the full procedure of ta'rif.

Key Terms
إباحة (ibaha): Permissibility to take and use without liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on taking a slaughtered camel near water in the desert?
A) It is always mubah to take and eat.
B) It is mubah only if the finder has a probable belief that the owner allowed it.
C) It is luqatah requiring announcement.
D) It is haram because the owner may return.

Key Takeaway
Items in water, desert camels, and wool from dead animals are often mubah, but careful attention to value and owner's presumed intent is required.

Guardian's Disposal of Minor's Luqatah: Announcement and Charity
Explains guardian's duty to announce and give sadaqah of minor's found property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the guardian's duty to announce a minor's luqatah and explain the permissibility of giving charity, identifying who bears liability.

Core Idea
Guardian may give charity of minor's found item, but liability falls on guardian, not minor.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author addresses a nuanced issue: what should a guardian do if a minor finds property? The text clarifies that announcement is obligatory, and while giving charity may seem harmful due to potential liability, a later resolution shows that the guardian bears personal responsibility. Thus, charity is permissible without harming the minor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage addresses the minor's luqatah and the guardian's duty to announce (ta'reef) is wajib.
The guardian may be the father (wali) or executor (wasi).
2
Analyze the initial concern
The author notes that giving charity might cause harm because if the owner appears and the item is gone, the guardian would have to compensate from the minor's property.
This would be detrimental to the minor.
3
Trace the resolution
Citing his own commentary, the author resolves that if the guardian gives charity and the owner later claims, the liability falls on the guardian personally, not on the minor's wealth.
This removes the harm, making charity permissible.
4
Note additional rulings on delegation
The passage also covers that the guardian may appoint another to announce if unable, but if he gives the item to someone without the judge's permission, he is liable.
Delegation is allowed with caution.
5
Summarize the practical outcome
Thus, the guardian may give charity after proper announcement, bearing personal liability; this protects the minor's property.
The mufta bihi position is the later opinion.

He remained silent about the ruling of making it the property of the minor if he was poor because it is known a fortiori. Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the luqatah (found property) of a minor. The author explains that if a minor finds an item, his guardian (wali) or executor (wasi) must announce it. They may then give it in charity on behalf of the minor. The author initially suggests that giving charity might not be allowed because if the owner later appears and demands compensation, the guardian would be liable, harming the minor. However, after citing a later opinion from the author's own commentary, it clarifies that if the guardian or executor gives charity and the owner later claims it, the liability falls on the guardian/executor personally, not on the minor. Thus, there is no harm, and it is permissible. The passage also discusses the definition of 'ta'reef' (announcement) and rules about delegation: the guardian may appoint someone else to announce if he cannot do it himself, but if he gives the item to someone without the judge's permission, he is liable.

Key Terms

الوصي (al-wasi): Executor appointed by the father or judge to manage minor's affairs.
التعريف (al-ta'reef): Announcement of found property in public places for a specific period.

Ruling on Items Obviously Abandoned by Owner
Discusses items known to be abandoned and rulings on taking and using them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between items obviously abandoned (mubah to use) and items requiring announcement (luqatah), and explain why ownership is not transferred by mere permission.

Core Idea
Throwing away an item indicates permission to use it, but ownership remains with the original owner.

When you find a date pit on the ground, can you just pick it up and use it? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on items that owners clearly abandon. The key is distinguishing between the owner's permission to use (ibaha) and actual transfer of ownership. We will explore the base ruling from al-Hidayah, the restriction to scattered items, and the application to various other found objects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
From al-Hidayah: if the found item is something the owner is known not to seek (like date pits or pomegranate peels), its discarding is considered permission (ibaha) to use without announcement.
2
Understand the restriction
Al-Bazzaziyya restricts this to scattered items. Collected piles of such items are treated as luqatah because they may be sought by the owner.
Check the distinction between scattered and collected.
3
Apply to other items
The passage extends the ruling to ears of grain, apples/pears in water, wood in water, a slaughtered camel in the desert, and a thrown carcass's wool. Each case turns on whether the item is presumed abandoned or still of value to the owner.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Sarakhsi did not mention the explicit permission at throwing; al-Bazzaziyya requires the scattered condition. The cautious position (mufta bihi) treats collected valuable items as luqatah.
Understand why the mufta bihi is the cautious view.
5
Derive the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: for items like date pits that are obviously abandoned and scattered, taking without announcement is mubah. For collected or valuable items, announcement is required.
This balances the original permissibility with protective caution.

Commentary
This is a ruling passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq about items that are clearly abandoned by their owner. The author explains that if the found property is something the owner is known not to seek (like a date pit or pomegranate peel), the act of throwing it away is considered permission (ibaha) to take and use it without announcement. However, ownership remains with the original owner because transferring ownership from an unknown person is invalid. If the owner later finds it in the taker's hand, he can reclaim it unless he explicitly said at the time of throwing that 'whoever takes it, it is his.' For each, there are nuanced rulings based on whether the item is presumed abandoned or still sought. The text also includes a commentary on the permissibility of appointing a deputy for announcement.

Key Terms
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permission to use an item without transferring ownership.
النواة (al-nawah): Date pit, used as an example of an item typically discarded and not sought.
اللقطة (al-luqatah): Found property that must be announced if it is of value and likely sought by owner.

Items obviously abandoned (e.g., date pits) are permissible to use without announcement, but ownership is not transferred unless explicit words of gift are spoken.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Qiyas vs Istihsan on Set-Free Animals
Explains dispute between qiyas and istihsan regarding owner's right to reclaim set-free animal after improvement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Muhammad opposed qiyas and preferred istihsan in the case of a set-free animal, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf of the dispute.

Core Idea
Istihsan overrides qiyas when analogy leads to immoral consequences — a set-free animal returns to its owner.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between those who apply strict analogy (qiyas) and those who follow juristic preference (istihsan) regarding a found emaciated animal. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's powerful objection demonstrates how Hanafi usul uses istihsan to prevent harmful outcomes, especially when the analogy could extend to human beings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on taking a set-free animal
Identify the basic rule: if the owner explicitly said 'I have made it for whoever takes it,' the taker owns it; otherwise, the owner can reclaim it. This is the starting point of the dispute.
The finder may improve the animal, but ownership depends on the owner's statement.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Examine the analogy: a set-free animal with no value is like discarded pomegranate peels. By qiyas, the taker should own it because the owner has abandoned any claim.
This is a jali qiyas based on the attribute of 'no value'.
3
Identify Imam Muhammad's istihsan objection
Imam Muhammad argues that if we allow qiyas for animals, we would have to allow it for slaves—a maimed female slave thrown away could be taken, improved, and then the taker could have intercourse without proper ownership. This is abominable (qabih).
This is a counter-argument using a reductio ad absurdum based on a stronger moral principle.
4
Recognise the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under qiyas, the taker owns the animal; under istihsan, the original owner can reclaim it.
The dispute is not about the ruling on taking (mubah) but about ownership after improvement.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The author of Al-Bahr al-Raiq cites Imam Muhammad's view as the preferred one. Istihsan is relied upon to prevent greater harm.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin, with tarjih by Imam Muhammad's strong reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he set free his riding animal and a person took it and improved it, then its owner came: if he said at the time of setting free 'I have made it for whoever takes it,' then the owner has no way to it because he permitted ownership. If he did not say that, he may take it. Similarly, if he released his hunted game. This is what some of our shaykhs chose. If they differ, the word is that of the owner with his oath that he did not say 'it is for whoever takes it' because he denies permission of ownership. If the taker provides proof or the owner refuses the oath, it is given to the taker. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth mentioned in his Nawazil: If there collects for the oil sellers what drips from the vessels into his container, then if it flows from outside the vessels, it is lawful for him because it is not for the buyer since what separates from them does not enter the sale. If it flows from inside or from both inside and outside combined, or he does not know, he considers: if the oil seller added from himself for each of the buyers, it is lawful for him; if he did not add, it is not lawful for him and he should give it in charity unless he is needy, for its way is the way of luqatah. End. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah: A man asked 'Ata' about a man who spent the night in the mosque and woke up with a purse containing dinars in his hand. He said: The one who put it in your hand did not put it except wanting to make it for you. End. In al-Zahirah: And whoever takes a falcon or similar in a city or countryside and on its legs are straps or bells, he must announce it because of certainty of another's hand over it before him. Similarly, if he took a deer with a necklace on its neck or a pigeon in the city that is known, for such are not wild, so if it was 'sarwalah' (?) he must announce it. End. His statement: (Then he gives charity) meaning if its owner does not come, he may give it in charity to the poor, not because it is due to the deserving, but it is wajib to the extent possible by delivering the essence when he finds its owner and delivering the compensation which is reward on the basis of his approval of giving charity. It will come that he may benefit from it, so he learns that he is given choice between them. He remained silent about keeping it, and he may do so hoping to find its owner as in al-Hidayah, and about giving it to the imam. In al-Khulasa: He raises the matter to the imam, and the imam has the choice: if he wishes he accepts, if he wishes he does not. If he accepts, if he wishes he hastens its charity, if he wishes he lends it to a trustworthy man, if he wishes he gives it as mudarabah, if he wishes he returns it to the finder. Then he has the choice: if he wishes he continues preservation, if he wishes he gives charity on the condition that the reward is for its owner, if he wishes he sells it if it is not dirhams or dinars and keeps its price, then after that... (the text about setting free his riding animal) He said in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: If a man's riding animal is emaciated and has no value, and he did not say at the time of abandoning 'let whoever wishes take it,' and a man took it and improved it, then by analogy (qiyas) it would be for the taker like thrown pomegranate peels, but by istihsan it belongs to its owner. Muhammad said: For if we allowed that in animals and made it for the taker, we would allow it in the female slave and male slave. They are thrown on the ground sick with no value, and a man takes them and spends on them until they become his property, then he has sexual intercourse with the female slave and acquires that without purchase, gift, inheritance, or charity. The boy without the owner giving ownership. This is an abominable thing. End. And by this is known the ruling of what al-Ramli mentioned, which is frequently asked about. (Al-Bahr al-Raiq, vol. 5, p. 175)
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage contains several rulings within the chapter of luqatah. The first discusses setting free an animal (sib al-dabbah). The finder may take and improve it. If the owner explicitly said 'I have made it for whoever takes it,' then the taker becomes owner and the owner cannot reclaim. Otherwise, the owner can reclaim it. If there is a dispute, the owner's word with oath is accepted unless the taker brings proof. This ruling is chosen by some Hanafi shaykhs. The passage then includes three other fatwas: (1) from Abu al-Layth about oil drippings from containers sold by oil sellers; (2) from 'Ata' about finding a purse in the mosque (the place suggests the owner intended to give it); (3) from al-Zahira about finding a falcon or deer with signs of ownership (requires announcement). The commentary then returns to the matn phrase 'thumma tasaddaq' (then he gives charity), explaining that if the owner does not appear, the finder may give it in charity to the poor, but this is not a right of the poor; it is a duty (wajib) to the extent possible, meaning he must try to deliver the item or its compensation (reward) if the owner later approves. He may also keep it or give it to the imam. The imam has various options (delay charity, lend, mudarabah, return to finder, or sell and keep price). Finally, the text returns to the animal case with a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute: Muhammad al-Shaybani argues against qiyas (which would give the animal to the taker like discarded peels) and for istihsan (return to owner) because allowing that for animals would set a precedent for slaves, leading to immoral consequences like intercourse without proper ownership transfer. This is a clear example of istihsan overriding qiyas to prevent harm.

Key Terms
التسييب (al-tasyib): Setting free an animal or abandoning it without a statement of permission.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here comparing found animal to discarded inanimate items.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; preferring a different ruling to avoid harm or injustice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad's reasoning in this passage, why should a found emaciated animal be returned to its owner rather than given to the finder?
A) Because the animal has no value
B) To prevent an analogy to slaves that could lead to immoral acts
C) Because the finder did not ask permission
D) Because the owner might come later

Key Takeaway
Istihsan overrides qiyas when the analogy would lead to immoral consequences, such as allowing ownership of found animals without proper transfer, especially when it could extend to humans.

Luqatah — Found Property
beginner
8 min
Taking a Lost Animal and the Owner's Statement of Abandonment
Opening ruling on found animals and the siyaba sub-case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking a lost animal and explain how the owner's explicit statement affects ownership when an animal is intentionally abandoned.

Core Idea
An abandoned animal remains with its owner unless he expressly gives it to whoever takes it.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: you find an animal that appears lost or abandoned. When can you keep it? The Hanafi school distinguishes between a simple found animal (which must be announced) and an animal the owner deliberately let go. The key is the owner's spoken intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, distinguish between a lost animal that the owner is unaware of (luqata) and an animal the owner intentionally abandons (siyaba). The rulings differ.
In luqata, the finder must announce; in siyaba, the owner's statement matters.
2
Analyze the effect of the owner's statement
If the owner says 'I make it for whoever takes it' (جعلتها لمن أخذها), the taker owns it. If he says nothing, the owner can reclaim it.
The statement is a legal permission (ibaha al-tamlik).
3
Apply the rule to disputes
If the owner denies making the statement, his word with oath is accepted because he denies permission. The taker must prove otherwise.
Burden of proof on the claimant (taker).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب اللقطة
ربها لا يطلبها ثم تصدق فإن جاء ربها نفذه أو ضمن الملتقط وصح التقاط البهيمة وهو على حاله فهو مباح فكل من أخذه فهو أولى، ولو سيب دابته فأخذها إنسان فأصلحها ثم جاء صاحبها فإن كان قال عند التسييب جعلتها لمن أخذها فلا سبيل لصاحبها عليها لأنه أباح التمليك، وإن لم يقل ذلك له أن يأخذها وكذلك من أرسل صيداً له هكذا اختاره بعض مشايخنا، فإن اختلفا فالقول قول صاحبها مع يمينه أنه لم يقل هي لمن أخذها لأنه ينكر إباحة التملك، وإن برهن الآخذ أو نكل المالك عن اليمين سلمت للآخذ. وذكر الفقيه أبو الليث في نوازله : إذا اجتمع للدهانين ما يقطر من الأوعية في إنائه، فإن كان يسيل من خارج الأوعية يطيب له لأنه ليس للمشتري لأن ما انفصل عنها لا يدخل البيع، وإن سال من الداخل أو من الداخل والخارج جميعاً أو لا يعلم ينظر ؛ إن زاد الدهان من عنده لكل واحد من المشترين طاب له، وإن لم يزد لا يطيب له ويتصدق به إلا أن يكون محتاجاً لأن سبيله سبيل اللقطة .اهـ. وفي التتارخانية : سأل رجل عطاء عن رجل بات في المسجد واستيقظ وفي يده صرة فيها دنانير قال : إن الذي صرها في يدك لم يصرها إلا وهو يريد أن يجعلها لك اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : ومن أخذ بازياً أو شبهه في مصر أو سواد وفي رجليه سير أو جلاجل فعليه أن يعرفه للتيقن بثبوت يد الغير عليه قبله وكذا لو أخذ ظبياً وفي عنقه قلادة أو حمامة في المصر يعرف إذ مثلها لا يكون وحشية بأن كانت مسرولة فعليه أن يعرفها اهـ . قوله : (سيب دابته الخ) قال في التتارخانية : ولو أن رجلاً ثاقب عليه دابته ولا قيمة لها من الهزال ولم يقل وقت الترك فليأخذها من شاء فأخذها رجل وأصلحها فالقياس أن يكون لأخذها كقشور الرمان المطروحة، وفي الاستحسان تكون لصاحبها قال محمد : لأنا لو جوزنا ذلك في الحيوان وجعلناه للأخذ لجوزنا في الجارية والعبد. ترمى في الأرض مريضة لا قيمة لها فيأخذه رجل وينفق عليه حتى ملكاً له فيطأ الجارية ويجد ذلك من غير شراء ولا هبة ولا إرث ولا صدقة الغلام من غير أن يملكه المالك وهذا أمر قبيح .اهـ. وبه علم حكم ما ذكره الرملي مما كثر السؤال عنه
Book of Found Property. Its owner does not seek it, then he gives charity; if its owner comes, he either enforces it or the finder is liable. And taking an animal is valid, and it is as it is, so it is permissible, so whoever takes it is more entitled. And if he let loose his animal and a person took it and repaired it, then its owner came: if he said at the time of letting loose 'I made it for whoever takes it,' then its owner has no claim over it because he permitted ownership. And if he did not say that, he has the right to take it, and likewise one who releases his game; thus some of our teachers chose it. If they differ, the statement is of its owner with his oath that he did not say 'it is for whoever takes it' because he denies permission of ownership. And if the taker proves or the owner refuses the oath, it is handed to the taker. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth mentioned in his Nawazil: If oil merchants collect what drips from vessels in his container, then if it flows from outside the vessels, it is lawful for him because it is not for the buyer, since what separates from them does not enter the sale. And if it flows from inside or from both inside and outside, or it is not known, he looks: if the oil merchant added from himself for each of the buyers, it is lawful; if he did not add, it is not lawful and he gives it in charity except if he is needy, because its way is the way of found property. End. And in al-TatarKhaniyya: A man asked 'Ata' about a person who slept in the mosque and woke up with a purse of dinars in his hand. He said: 'The one who tied it in your hand did not tie it except that he intended to make it for you.' End. And in al-Zahiriyya: Whoever takes a falcon or similar in a city or rural area, and on its legs are a strap or bells, he must announce it because of certainty of the hand of another on it before him. Similarly, if he takes a gazelle with a necklace on its neck, or a pigeon in the city that is known, since its like cannot be wild, i.e., it was wearing trousers, he must announce it. End. His statement: (He let loose his animal, etc.) He said in al-TatarKhaniyya: If a man's animal is in a critical condition and has no value due to emaciation, and he did not say at the time of leaving 'let whoever wishes take it,' and a man took it and repaired it, then by qiyas it would be for the taker like discarded pomegranate peels, but by istihsan it belongs to its owner. Muhammad said: because if we allowed that in animals and made it for the taker, we would allow it in a slave-girl or a slave: they are thrown on the ground sick with no value, so a man takes them and spends on them until he owns them, then he has intercourse with the slave-girl, and he finds that without purchase, gift, inheritance, or charity; the boy without being owned by the owner, and this is an ugly matter. End. And by this is known the ruling of what al-Ramli mentioned about what is frequently asked.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage from البحر الرائق is a commentary on the matn of كنز الدقائق discussing various rulings related to found property (لقطة). It opens with the ruling that if someone finds an animal, it is permissible to take it, and whoever takes it is more entitled (أولى). The text then addresses a specific sub-issue: if a person intentionally abandons his animal (سيب دابته), and another person takes it and cares for it, then the original owner's rights depend on whether he explicitly said at the time of abandonment that he makes it for whoever takes it. If he did, the taker owns it; if not, the owner can reclaim it. This is based on the principle that the owner's statement permits ownership (إباحة التمليك). The commentary includes a qiyas vs. istihsan discussion: qiyas would treat the abandoned animal like discarded pomegranate peels (ownerless), but istihsan returns it to the owner to prevent the ugly consequence of allowing ownership of a living being without proper transfer (like a slave). Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) argues that istihsan prevents the analogy to inanimate objects because it would lead to allowing intercourse with a found slave-girl without purchase, gift, inheritance, or charity, which is repugnant. The passage also includes subsidiary rulings: drippings from oil merchants' vessels (if from outside, it belongs to the dripper; if from inside, it is charity unless the dealer added his own oil), a found purse in a mosque (presumed gift from the one who placed it), and animals with marks (must be announced because likely previously owned). The final line notes that this answers a common question from al-Ramli.

Key Terms
لقطة (luqata): Property found by someone, which must be announced and preserved for the owner
سيب (sayyaba): To abandon or let loose an animal intentionally
إباحة التمليك (ibaha al-tamlik): Permitting ownership, i.e., a statement that makes the item owned by whoever takes it

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man abandons his animal without saying anything, and another takes it and cares for it, what is the ruling?
A) The animal becomes property of the taker.
B) The animal remains property of the original owner.
C) The animal must be given in charity.
D) The animal is considered lost property and must be announced for a year.

Key Takeaway
An intentionally abandoned animal remains with its owner unless he explicitly says 'I make it for whoever takes it'.

Luqatah — Found Property

Qiyas vs Istihsan: The Emaciated Abandoned Animal
The qiyas vs istihsan argument from Imam Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the qiyas and istihsan arguments for an abandoned emaciated animal and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Qiyas would make an abandoned animal ownerless, but istihsan returns it to the owner to prevent repugnant consequences.

This lesson dives into a legal dispute within the Hanafi school. When an animal is so emaciated that it has no value, is it like discarded pomegranate peels (ownerless)? Imam Muhammad rejects this analogy because it would lead to an ugly outcome: if applied to a slave girl, the taker could own and have intercourse with her without a valid contract. Istihsan overrides qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Understand the qiyas analogy
The asl (original case): discarded pomegranate peels become ownerless. The far' (new case): an emaciated abandoned animal. The illa (effective cause): both are discarded with no indication of continued ownership. By qiyas, the animal would become ownerless and belong to the taker.
Qiyas is a valid method, but here it is rejected.
2
Identify the objection based on istihsan
Imam Muhammad objects: if we apply qiyas here, we must also apply it to an abandoned slave girl or boy. That would allow the taker to own them without proper transfer and, in the case of a female slave, have intercourse with her without purchase, gift, inheritance, or charity. This is repugnant (قبيح).
The argument is from a stronger evil.
3
Conclude with the mufta bihi
Because of this repugnant consequence, istihsan overrides qiyas. The animal remains the property of its original owner. This is the relied-upon position.
Imam Muhammad's statement is taken as authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب اللقطة
ربها لا يطلبها ثم تصدق فإن جاء ربها نفذه أو ضمن الملتقط وصح التقاط البهيمة وهو على حاله فهو مباح فكل من أخذه فهو أولى، ولو سيب دابته فأخذها إنسان فأصلحها ثم جاء صاحبها فإن كان قال عند التسييب جعلتها لمن أخذها فلا سبيل لصاحبها عليها لأنه أباح التمليك، وإن لم يقل ذلك له أن يأخذها وكذلك من أرسل صيداً له هكذا اختاره بعض مشايخنا، فإن اختلفا فالقول قول صاحبها مع يمينه أنه لم يقل هي لمن أخذها لأنه ينكر إباحة التملك، وإن برهن الآخذ أو نكل المالك عن اليمين سلمت للآخذ. وذكر الفقيه أبو الليث في نوازله : إذا اجتمع للدهانين ما يقطر من الأوعية في إنائه، فإن كان يسيل من خارج الأوعية يطيب له لأنه ليس للمشتري لأن ما انفصل عنها لا يدخل البيع، وإن سال من الداخل أو من الداخل والخارج جميعاً أو لا يعلم ينظر ؛ إن زاد الدهان من عنده لكل واحد من المشترين طاب له، وإن لم يزد لا يطيب له ويتصدق به إلا أن يكون محتاجاً لأن سبيله سبيل اللقطة .اهـ. وفي التتارخانية : سأل رجل عطاء عن رجل بات في المسجد واستيقظ وفي يده صرة فيها دنانير قال : إن الذي صرها في يدك لم يصرها إلا وهو يريد أن يجعلها لك اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : ومن أخذ بازياً أو شبهه في مصر أو سواد وفي رجليه سير أو جلاجل فعليه أن يعرفه للتيقن بثبوت يد الغير عليه قبله وكذا لو أخذ ظبياً وفي عنقه قلادة أو حمامة في المصر يعرف إذ مثلها لا يكون وحشية بأن كانت مسرولة فعليه أن يعرفها اهـ . قوله : (سيب دابته الخ) قال في التتارخانية : ولو أن رجلاً ثاقب عليه دابته ولا قيمة لها من الهزال ولم يقل وقت الترك فليأخذها من شاء فأخذها رجل وأصلحها فالقياس أن يكون لأخذها كقشور الرمان المطروحة، وفي الاستحسان تكون لصاحبها قال محمد : لأنا لو جوزنا ذلك في الحيوان وجعلناه للأخذ لجوزنا في الجارية والعبد. ترمى في الأرض مريضة لا قيمة لها فيأخذه رجل وينفق عليه حتى ملكاً له فيطأ الجارية ويجد ذلك من غير شراء ولا هبة ولا إرث ولا صدقة الغلام من غير أن يملكه المالك وهذا أمر قبيح .اهـ. وبه علم حكم ما ذكره الرملي مما كثر السؤال عنه

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas to avoid harsh or repugnant outcomes
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause
قشور الرمان (qushur al-rumman): Pomegranate peels, used as an example of discarded items that become ownerless by custom

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does istihsan override qiyas in the case of an abandoned emaciated animal?
A) Because the owner might come back later.
B) Because the qiyas analogy would lead to repugnant consequences if applied to slaves.
C) Because the animal is too valuable to be taken.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan prevents an analogy that would produce a repugnant result, preserving the principles of legitimate ownership and intimacy.

Luqatah — Found Property
intermediate

Subsidiary Rulings: Oil Drippings, Found Purse, and Marked Animals
Three subsidiary rulings from the latter part of the passage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rulings for drippings from vessels, a found purse in a mosque, and animals with identifying marks.

Core Idea
Subsidiary cases of found property are judged by the principle of appearance: outward evidence dictates the ruling unless proven otherwise.

The passage includes three additional rulings that illustrate how the principles of luqata and ownership apply in specific contexts: drippings from oil merchants' containers, a purse found in a mosque, and animals with marks like a falcon with bells. Each has its own reasoning based on custom and presumption of ownership.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the oil drippings case
If drippings come from outside the vessels, they belong to the collector because they are not part of the buyer's purchase. If from inside, they are luqata and must be given in charity unless the merchant added oil from his own stock.
Custom and contract terms determine the ruling.
2
Analyze the found purse in mosque
Based on a statement from 'Ata', the one who placed the purse in the sleeper's hand presumably intended to give it to him. So it is a gift, not luqata.
Presumption of intention based on the act.
3
Analyze animals with marks
If a falcon has straps or bells, or a gazelle has a necklace, or a pigeon is domesticated (wearing 'trousers'), they must be announced because they are likely owned. No presumption of ownerlessness.
The mark creates certainty (تيقن) of prior ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب اللقطة
ربها لا يطلبها ثم تصدق فإن جاء ربها نفذه أو ضمن الملتقط وصح التقاط البهيمة وهو على حاله فهو مباح فكل من أخذه فهو أولى، ولو سيب دابته فأخذها إنسان فأصلحها ثم جاء صاحبها فإن كان قال عند التسييب جعلتها لمن أخذها فلا سبيل لصاحبها عليها لأنه أباح التمليك، وإن لم يقل ذلك له أن يأخذها وكذلك من أرسل صيداً له هكذا اختاره بعض مشايخنا، فإن اختلفا فالقول قول صاحبها مع يمينه أنه لم يقل هي لمن أخذها لأنه ينكر إباحة التملك، وإن برهن الآخذ أو نكل المالك عن اليمين سلمت للآخذ. وذكر الفقيه أبو الليث في نوازله : إذا اجتمع للدهانين ما يقطر من الأوعية في إنائه، فإن كان يسيل من خارج الأوعية يطيب له لأنه ليس للمشتري لأن ما انفصل عنها لا يدخل البيع، وإن سال من الداخل أو من الداخل والخارج جميعاً أو لا يعلم ينظر ؛ إن زاد الدهان من عنده لكل واحد من المشترين طاب له، وإن لم يزد لا يطيب له ويتصدق به إلا أن يكون محتاجاً لأن سبيله سبيل اللقطة .اهـ. وفي التتارخانية : سأل رجل عطاء عن رجل بات في المسجد واستيقظ وفي يده صرة فيها دنانير قال : إن الذي صرها في يدك لم يصرها إلا وهو يريد أن يجعلها لك اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : ومن أخذ بازياً أو شبهه في مصر أو سواد وفي رجليه سير أو جلاجل فعليه أن يعرفه للتيقن بثبوت يد الغير عليه قبله وكذا لو أخذ ظبياً وفي عنقه قلادة أو حمامة في المصر يعرف إذ مثلها لا يكون وحشية بأن كانت مسرولة فعليه أن يعرفها اهـ . قوله : (سيب دابته الخ) قال في التتارخانية : ولو أن رجلاً ثاقب عليه دابته ولا قيمة لها من الهزال ولم يقل وقت الترك فليأخذها من شاء فأخذها رجل وأصلحها فالقياس أن يكون لأخذها كقشور الرمان المطروحة، وفي الاستحسان تكون لصاحبها قال محمد : لأنا لو جوزنا ذلك في الحيوان وجعلناه للأخذ لجوزنا في الجارية والعبد. ترمى في الأرض مريضة لا قيمة لها فيأخذه رجل وينفق عليه حتى ملكاً له فيطأ الجارية ويجد ذلك من غير شراء ولا هبة ولا إرث ولا صدقة الغلام من غير أن يملكه المالك وهذا أمر قبيح .اهـ. وبه علم حكم ما ذكره الرملي مما كثر السؤال عنه
Book of Found Property. Its owner does not seek it, then he gives charity; if its owner comes, he either enforces it or the finder is liable. And taking an animal is valid, and it is as it is, so it is permissible, so whoever takes it is more entitled. And if he let loose his animal and a person took it and repaired it, then its owner came: if he said at the time of letting loose 'I made it for whoever takes it,' then its owner has no claim over it because he permitted ownership. And if he did not say that, he has the right to take it, and likewise one who releases his game; thus some of our teachers chose it. If they differ, the statement is of its owner with his oath that he did not say 'it is for whoever takes it' because he denies permission of ownership. And if the taker proves or the owner refuses the oath, it is handed to the taker. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth mentioned in his Nawazil: If oil merchants collect what drips from vessels in his container, then if it flows from outside the vessels, it is lawful for him because it is not for the buyer, since what separates from them does not enter the sale. And if it flows from inside or from both inside and outside, or it is not known, he looks: if the oil merchant added from himself for each of the buyers, it is lawful; if he did not add, it is not lawful and he gives it in charity except if he is needy, because its way is the way of found property. End. And in al-TatarKhaniyya: A man asked 'Ata' about a person who slept in the mosque and woke up with a purse of dinars in his hand. He said: 'The one who tied it in your hand did not tie it except that he intended to make it for you.' End. And in al-Zahiriyya: Whoever takes a falcon or similar in a city or rural area, and on its legs are a strap or bells, he must announce it because of certainty of the hand of another on it before him. Similarly, if he takes a gazelle with a necklace on its neck, or a pigeon in the city that is known, since its like cannot be wild, i.e., it was wearing trousers, he must announce it. End. His statement: (He let loose his animal, etc.) He said in al-TatarKhaniyya: If a man's animal is in a critical condition and has no value due to emaciation, and he did not say at the time of leaving 'let whoever wishes take it,' and a man took it and repaired it, then by qiyas it would be for the taker like discarded pomegranate peels, but by istihsan it belongs to its owner. Muhammad said: because if we allowed that in animals and made it for the taker, we would allow it in a slave-girl or a slave: they are thrown on the ground sick with no value, so a man takes them and spends on them until he owns them, then he has intercourse with the slave-girl, and he finds that without purchase, gift, inheritance, or charity; the boy without being owned by the owner, and this is an ugly matter. End. And by this is known the ruling of what al-Ramli mentioned about what is frequently asked.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage from البحر الرائق is a commentary on the matn of كنز الدقائق discussing various rulings related to found property (لقطة). It opens with the ruling that if someone finds an animal, it is permissible to take it, and whoever takes it is more entitled (أولى). The text then addresses a specific sub-issue: if a person intentionally abandons his animal (سيب دابته), and another person takes it and cares for it, then the original owner's rights depend on whether he explicitly said at the time of abandonment that he makes it for whoever takes it. If he did, the taker owns it; if not, the owner can reclaim it. This is based on the principle that the owner's statement permits ownership (إباحة التمليك). The commentary includes a qiyas vs. istihsan discussion: qiyas would treat the abandoned animal like discarded pomegranate peels (ownerless), but istihsan returns it to the owner to prevent the ugly consequence of allowing ownership of a living being without proper transfer (like a slave). Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) argues that istihsan prevents the analogy to inanimate objects because it would lead to allowing intercourse with a found slave-girl without purchase, gift, inheritance, or charity, which is repugnant. The passage also includes subsidiary rulings: drippings from oil merchants' vessels (if from outside, it belongs to the dripper; if from inside, it is charity unless the dealer added his own oil), a found purse in a mosque (presumed gift from the one who placed it), and animals with marks (must be announced because likely previously owned). The final line notes that this answers a common question from al-Ramli.

Key Terms
نوازل (nawazil): A genre of legal literature dealing with emergent cases
صرة (surrah): A purse or bundle of money
مسرولة (masrulan): A pigeon that wears 'trousers' (a cloth tied on its feet, a sign of domestication)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should be done with oil that drips from inside the vessels and the merchant did not add his own oil?
A) It is permissible to keep it.
B) It must be given in charity unless the collector is needy.
C) It must be returned to the customers.
D) It can be sold and the profit kept.

Key Takeaway
Context and visible signs determine whether found items are treated as gifts, luqata, or permissible acquisitions.

Luqatah — Found Property
intermediate
8 min
Finder's Options: Charity, Sale, and Return of Owner
Expansion on finder's charity, sale, and owner's ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the finder's options after announcement and explain the owner's right to ratify the charity or hold the finder liable.

Core Idea
The finder's charity is provisional: the owner can ratify it for reward or demand compensation.

After proper announcement, the finder (multaqit) has several lawful options: give charity to the poor, keep the item, hand it to the imam, or sell non-monetary items. The imam also has discretion. Yet all these disposals are subject to the owner's ultimate right: he may ratify the charity and gain the reward, or hold the finder liable for the value. This lesson unpacks the rulings and the underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the finder's permissible acts
From the text, list the actions the finder may take after announcement: giving charity, keeping, handing to imam, selling non-monetary items.
Charity is obligatory to the extent possible, but the finder has a choice among options.
2
Understand the imam's role and options
When the finder refers to the imam, the imam may accept or decline; if he accepts, he can give charity, lend, invest as mudaraba, or return to finder.
The imam acts as a supervisor (nazir) in the owner's interest.
3
Analyze the owner's right upon return
If the owner appears after charity, he may either ratify the charity and receive the reward, or hold the finder liable for the value. This is because the charity lacks the owner's direct permission, even if permitted by the Law.
Ratification is possible even after the item perishes, unlike a fuduli sale.
4
Distinguish charity from fuduli sale
A fuduli sale requires the item to exist for ratification, because ownership transfers only after ratification. In charity, ownership vests in the poor before ratification, so ratification does not need the item.
This difference stems from the nature of the act: charity is a gratuitous transfer with a spiritual reward.
5
Note special cases and ikhtilaf
Charity cannot be given to a rich person, a rich slave, or a rich minor. If the item belongs to a dhimmi, it goes to the public treasury. If the finder dies, he must bequeath the item; heirs must announce, else they may be liable (Abu Yusuf has a different view).
These detail the boundaries of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثم تصدق أي إن لم يجيء صاحبها فله أن يتصدق بها على الفقراء أيضاً لا للحق إلى المستحق وهو واجب بقدر الإمكان وذلك بإيصال عينها عند الظفر بصاحبها وإيصال العوض وهو الثواب على اعتبار إجازته التصدق بها وسيأتي أن له أن ينتفع بها فعلم أنه مخير بينهما وسكت عن إمساكها وله ذلك رجاء الظفر بصاحبها كما في الهداية، وعن دفعها للإمام. قال في الخلاصة : يرفع الأمر إلى الإمام والإمام بالخيار إن شاء قبل وإن شاء لم يقبل، فإن قبل إن شاء عجل صدقتها وإن شاء أقرضها من رجل مليء وإن شاء دفعها مضاربة وإن شاء ردها على الملتقط، ثم هو بالخيار إن شاء أدام الحفظ وإن شاء تصدق على أن يكون الثواب لصاحبها، وإن شاء باعها إن لم تكن دراهم أو دنانير وأمسك ثمنها ثم بعد ذلك إن حضر مالكها ليس له نقض البيع إن كان البيع بأمر القاضي وإن باع بغير أمر القاضي وهي قائمة فإن شاء أجاز البيع وأخذ الثمن، وإن شاء أبطل البيع وأخذ عين ماله، وإن هلكت إن شاء ضمن البائع وعند ذلك ينفذ البيع من جهة البائع في ظاهر الرواية وبه أخذ عامة المشايخ. وذكر الإمام السرخسي أن المودع إذا باع الوديعة وهلكت وضمنه المالك فهو كالملتقط اهـ. وفي الذخيرة والحاصل أن الإمام يصير ناظراً فيفعل مايراه أصلح في حق صاحب اللقطة اهـ. وفي الحاوي : الدفع بعد الإشهاد إلى القاضي أجود ليفعل القاضي الأصلح. وفي المجتبى والتصدق بيده في زماننا أولى من الدفع إلى الحاكم، وقد مر في كتاب التوبة لقاضي القضاة عبد الجبار المتكلم أن الواجب فيها أن يتصدق بنفسه ولا يلقيه في يد غيره لأنه لا يعلم هل يؤديها إلى مستحقها أو لا اهـ. وقيدنا بالتصدق على الفقراء لما في الهداية أنه لا يتصدق باللقطة على غني - زاد في الحاوي - ولا مملوك غني ولا ولد غني صغير، واستثنى من التصدق باللقطة ما إذا عرف أنها لذمي فلا يتصدق بها وكانت في بيت المال للنوائب ؛ كذا في التتارخانية وفي القنية وما يتصدق به الملتقط بعد التعريف وغلبة يوجد صاحبه لا يجب إيصاؤه وإن كان يرجو وجود المالك وجب الإيصاء اهـ . وإذا أمسكها وخشي الموت يوصي بها كيلا تدخل في الميراث ثم الورثة أيضاً يعرفونها، ومقتضى النظر أنهم لو لم يعرفوها حتى هلكت وجاء صاحبها أن يضمنوا لأنهم وضعوا أيديهم على لقطة ولم يشهدوا أي لم يعرفوا ويغلب على الظن بذلك أن قصدهم تعميتها ويجري فيهم خلاف أبي يوسف ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقد يقال : إن التعريف عليهم غير واجب حيث عرفها الملتقط . قوله : (فإن جاء ربها نفذه أو ضمن الملتقط أي إن جاء مالكها بعد تصدق الملتقط خير بين إمضاء الصدقة والثواب له وبين تضمين الملتقط لأن التصدق وإن حصل بإذن الشرع لم يحصل بإذنه فيتوقف على إجازته أطلق في التنفيذ فشمل ما بعد هلاك العين لأن الملك يثبت للفقير قبل الإجازة فلا يتوقف على قيام المحل بخلاف بيع الفضولي فإنه يشترط لصحة إجازته قيام العين لثبوت الملك بعد الإجازة فيه، وإما تضمين الملتقط فلكونه سلم ماله إلى غيره بغير إذنه إلا أنه بإباحة من جهة الشرع وهذا لا ينافي الضمان حقاً للعبد كما في تناول مال الغير حالة المخمصة. وأطلق فيه فشمل ما إذا كان التصدق بأمر القاضي وهو الصحيح لأن أمره لا يكون أعلى من فعله والقاضي لو تصدق بها كان له أن يضمنه فكذا له أن يضمن من أمره القاضي ولذا أطلق المصنف في الملتقط فشمل القاضي، ولذا قال في الذخيرة: وإذا مال الحاج وغيره إذا أعيا بعيره تركه فيأخذه غيره حتى عاد لحاله قوله : (وفي المجتبى والتصدق بيده في زماننا أولى) قال في النهر : وينبغي أن يفصل في القاضي إن غلب على ظنه ورعه وعدم
His statement: (Then he gives charity) meaning if its owner does not come, he may give it in charity to the poor, not to the one who is entitled [to zakat], and it is obligatory to the extent possible, by delivering the actual property when he finds its owner and delivering the compensation which is reward, based on the owner's ratification of the charity. And it will come that he may benefit from it, so it is known that he has a choice between the two, and he remained silent about keeping it, and he has that right hoping to find its owner, as in al-Hidaya. And regarding handing it to the imam. He said in al-Khulasa: The matter is raised to the imam, and the imam has a choice: if he wishes he accepts, if he wishes he does not accept. If he accepts, if he wishes he hastens its charity, if he wishes he lends it to a trustworthy man, if he wishes he gives it as mudaraba, if he wishes he returns it to the finder. Then the finder has a choice: if he wishes he continues preservation, if he wishes he gives charity on condition that the reward is for its owner, if he wishes he sells it if it is not dirhams or dinars and keeps its price. Then after that, if its owner comes, he cannot nullify the sale if the sale was by order of the qadi. And if he sold without the order of the qadi while it is still existing, then if he wishes he ratifies the sale and takes the price, if he wishes he nullifies the sale and takes his actual property. And if it perishes, if he wishes he holds the seller liable, and then the sale is executed from the seller's side in the apparent narration, and the majority of shaykhs adopted it. Imam al-Sarakhsi mentioned that if the trustee sells the deposit and it perishes and the owner holds him liable, it is like the finder. End. In al-Dhakhira: The conclusion is that the imam becomes a supervisor and does what he sees as most beneficial for the owner of the found property. End. In al-Hawi: Handing it over after giving testimony to the qadi is better so that the qadi does what is most beneficial. In al-Mujtaba: Giving charity by his own hand in our time is better than handing it to the ruler. And it has passed in the Book of Repentance for Qadi al-Qudat 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Mutakallim that the obligatory in it is to give charity himself and not put it in the hand of another because he does not know whether that other will deliver it to its rightful recipient or not. End. We restricted charity to the poor because in al-Hidaya it says that one does not give charity from the found property to a rich person. Al-Hawi added: nor to the rich slave, nor to the rich minor child. And an exception is made from charity of found property when it is known that it belongs to a dhimmi, then he does not give charity with it, and it goes to the public treasury for incidents. Thus in al-TatarKhaniyya and al-Qunyah. And what the finder gives in charity after announcement and it is likely that its owner will not be found, it is not obligatory to will it. But if he hopes to find the owner, he must will it. End. And if he keeps it and fears death, he bequeaths it so that it does not enter inheritance, then the heirs also announce it. And the requirement of consideration is that if they do not announce it until it perishes and its owner comes, they are held liable because they placed their hands on a found property and did not give testimony, i.e., they did not announce, and it is mostly thought that their intention is to conceal it. And there is a difference of opinion from Abu Yusuf regarding them. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And it may be said: Announcement is not obligatory on them since the finder already announced it. His statement: (Then if its owner comes, he either enforces it or holds the finder liable) meaning if its owner comes after the finder gave charity, he has a choice between ratifying the charity and taking the reward, and holding the finder liable. Because the charity, although done by permission of the Law, was not done by his permission, so it depends on his ratification. He (the author) made the enforcement absolute, so it includes after the destruction of the actual property because ownership is established for the poor before ratification, so it does not depend on existence of the subject matter, unlike the sale of a fuduli (unauthorized agent), for the validity of his ratification requires the existence of the subject matter because ownership is established after ratification. As for holding the finder liable, it is because he handed over his property to another without his permission, except that it is permitted by the Law, and this does not negate liability as a right of the servant, like consuming another's property in the case of dire necessity. He made it absolute, so it includes if the charity was by order of the qadi, and this is correct because his order is not higher than his own action, and if the qadi had given charity with it, he could hold him liable, so too he can hold liable the one whom the qadi ordered. Hence the author made the finder absolute, including the qadi. Hence it is said in al-Dhakhira: And when the pilgrim's camel tires, he leaves it and another takes it until it returns to its state. His statement: (And in al-Mujtaba: Charity by his hand in our time is better) He said in al-Nahr: And it should be distinguished regarding the qadi: if it is likely that he is pious and not...
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe (شرح) on the matn's phrase 'ثم تصدق' (then give charity) and the subsequent clause 'فإن جاء ربها نفذه أو ضمن الملتقط' (if its owner comes, he either enforces it or holds the finder liable). It details the finder's options after proper announcement: he may give charity to the poor on behalf of the owner, keep the item hoping to find the owner, or hand it to the imam (ruler/judge). The imam has several options: he can give immediate charity, lend it, invest it as mudaraba, or return it to the finder. The finder may also sell non-monetary items and keep the price. If the owner appears, he can ratify the charity and get the reward, or hold the finder liable for the value. The passage notes that giving charity personally is better than involving the judge in later times. It also addresses special cases: charity cannot be given to a rich person, to a rich slave, or to a rich minor; if the item belongs to a dhimmi, it cannot be given in charity but goes to the public treasury. If the finder dies, he must bequeath the found property to avoid it entering his inheritance; his heirs must announce it. There is a discussion on liability if heirs fail to announce. The key legal principle is that the finder's charity is effective but depends on the owner's ratification, unlike a sale by an unauthorized agent (فضولي) which requires the item to exist for ratification. The finder can be held liable because he disposed of another's property without permission, though the Law permits it under necessity.

Key Terms
ملتقط (multaqit): The person who finds and picks up lost property
تصدق (tasaddaqa): To give in charity on behalf of the owner, with the intention that the reward goes to the owner
فضولي (fuduli): An unauthorized agent who contracts on behalf of another without prior permission

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the finder gives charity with the judge's order, and then the owner appears, can the owner hold the finder liable?
A) No, because the order of the judge provides immunity.
B) Yes, because the judge's order does not negate the owner's right to compensation.
C) Only if the judge was unjust.
D) Only if the charity was not given to the poor.

Key Takeaway
The finder's charity is provisional: the owner can either ratify and gain the reward, or demand compensation from the finder, even if the charity was by the imam's order.

Luqatah — Found Property
beginner
10 min
Owner's Options When Finder Sells Luqatah
Ruling on sale of luqatah by finder with and without qadi's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the owner's options when the finder sells the luqatah, distinguishing between sale with and without qadi's permission, and apply the ruling to scenarios where the item is extant or perished.

Core Idea
A sale of luqatah without qadi's permission gives the owner a choice: ratify and take the price, or nullify and reclaim the item.

This ruling from the Hanafi school clarifies the legal consequences when a finder sells a lost item before the owner appears. The owner's rights depend on whether the sale was authorized by the qadi. If authorized, the sale is binding; if not, the owner has options. Understanding this protects both the owner's property rights and the finder's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, distinguish between the sale conducted with the permission of the qadi and the sale without it. The ruling differs fundamentally based on this condition.
The qadi's permission makes the sale binding on the owner.
2
Determine the owner's right in each scenario
If the sale was with qadi's permission, the owner has no right to rescind. If without, the owner has a choice.
3
Examine the subcases for sale without permission
When the sale was without permission, check if the item still exists or has perished. The owner's options depend on this.
If the item is extant, the owner can ratify the sale and take the price, or nullify it and take the item. If perished, the owner can only claim compensation from the seller.
4
Understand the Hanafi position on execution of sale
Note that according to Zahir al-Riwaya, the sale is considered executed from the seller's side (the finder) once the owner chooses compensation, meaning the sale becomes effective retrospectively.
This is the view of the majority of mashayikh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب اللقطة : حضر مالكها ليس له نقض البيع إن كان البيع بأمر القاضي وإن باع بغير أمر القاضي وهي قائمة فإن شاء أجاز البيع وأخذ الثمن، وإن شاء أبطل البيع وأخذ عين ماله، وإن هلكت إن شاء ضمن البائع وعند ذلك ينفذ البيع من جهة البائع في ظاهر الرواية وبه أخذ عامة المشايخ.
Book of Luqatah: If its owner comes, he has no right to rescind the sale if the sale was by order of the qadi. If he sold without the qadi's order and the item is still in existence, then if he wishes, he may ratify the sale and take the price, and if he wishes, he may nullify the sale and take his actual property. If it has perished, then if he wishes, he may hold the seller liable for compensation. And at that point, the sale is executed from the seller's side in the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwaya), and most of the mashayikh adopted this.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on the situation where the owner of a lost item (luqatah) appears after the finder has sold it. The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) if the sale was conducted with the permission of the qadi, the owner has no right to rescind the sale; (2) if the sale was without the qadi's permission and the item is still extant, the owner has a choice: he may either ratify the sale and take the price, or nullify the sale and reclaim the actual item. If the item has perished, the owner may opt to hold the seller (the finder) liable for compensation. The final statement indicates that according to the well-known transmission (Zahir al-Riwaya) and the practice of the majority of scholars, the sale is considered executed from the seller's side once the owner receives compensation. The passage is a direct ruling with no further proof cited.

Key Terms
اللقطة (al-luqatah): Lost property picked up by a finder
القاضي (al-qadi): The judge authorized to oversee the disposal of luqatah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the owner's right if the finder sold the luqatah without the qadi's permission and the item is still in existence?
A) He can only nullify the sale and take the item.
B) He can only ratify the sale and take the price.
C) He can choose either to ratify the sale and take the price, or nullify it and take the item.
D) He cannot do anything because the finder acted legally.

Key Takeaway
The owner's consent is crucial in unauthorized sale of luqatah; with qadi's permission, the sale is binding.

Charity of Luqatah: Owner's Options When He Appears
Ruling on finder's charity and owner's right to enforce or demand compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to the owner of a luqatah after the finder has given it in charity, explain the legal reasoning behind the finder's liability, and identify the mufta bihi position regarding the poor person's liability.

Core Idea
Charity of luqatah is valid only with owner's ratification; otherwise he can demand compensation from the finder.

In the law of luqatah, after announcing the found item, the finder may give it in charity if he fears it will perish. But what happens when the owner reappears? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the owner's rights: he can either validate the charity and gain reward, or hold the finder liable for compensation. The ruling applies even if the charity was done by order of the qadi, and there is a within-school difference about whether the owner can also hold the poor recipient liable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the owner's options
The finder has given the found item in charity. The owner appears. Identify the two options the owner has according to the text: enforce the charity and get reward, or hold the finder liable.
2
Analyze the legal rationale for the finder's liability
The charity was done with legal permission but without the owner's consent. The text analogizes it to consuming another's property during dire necessity: the act is permitted, but liability remains. Identify the illa (effective cause) that justifies compensation.
The illa is 'disposal of another's property without his permission, though permitted by the Law'.
3
Extend the ruling to charity done by qadi's order
The ruling includes cases where the charity was done by order of the qadi. The qadi's order does not elevate his authority above his own act; thus the owner can still hold the finder liable.
The author uses the word 'finder' generically to include the qadi if he acted directly.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the poor person's liability
The author notes that 'they said' the owner may also choose to hold the poor person liable. Determine which view is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) and why.
The correct view is that only the finder is liable; the poor person took in good faith.
5
Determine the consequences of each choice
If the owner enforces the charity, the finder gets the reward. If he holds the finder liable, the finder owns the item from the time of taking and must compensate; the poor person keeps the item but ownership retroactively transfers to the finder.
If the item still exists, the owner can take it directly from the poor person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن جاء ربها نفذه أو ضمن الملتقط) أي إن جاء مالكها بعد تصدق الملتقط خير بين إمضاء الصدقة والثواب له وبين تضمين الملتقط لأن التصدق وإن حصل بإذن الشرع لم يحصل بإذنه فيتوقف على إجازته أطلق في التنفيذ فشمل ما بعد هلاك العين لأن الملك يثبت للفقير قبل الإجازة فلا يتوقف على قيام المحل بخلاف بيع الفضولي فإنه يشترط لصحة إجازته قيام العين لثبوت الملك بعد الإجازة فيه، وإما تضمين الملتقط فلكونه سلم ماله إلى غيره بغير إذنه إلا أنه بإباحة من جهة الشرع وهذا لا ينافي الضمان حقاً للعبد كما في تناول مال الغير حالة المخمصة. وأطلق فيه فشمل ما إذا كان التصدق بأمر القاضي وهو الصحيح لأن أمره لا يكون أعلى من فعله والقاضي لو تصدق بها كان له أن يضمنه فكذا له أن يضمن من أمره القاضي ولذا أطلق المصنف في الملتقط فشمل القاضي. ولم يذكر المصنف تضمين المسكين قالوا : إنه مخير إن شاء ضمن الملتقط وإن شاء ضمن المسكين وأيهما ضمن لا يرجع على صاحبه، فإن ضمن الملتقط ملكها الملتقط من وقت الأخذ ويكون الثواب له، وإن كانت العين قائمة أخذها من يد الفقير ؛ كذا في الخانية وبه علم أن الثواب موقوف.
His statement: 'If its owner comes, he enforces it or holds the finder liable' means: if the owner comes after the finder has given charity, he has a choice between enforcing the charity and getting the reward, or holding the finder liable for compensation. Because the charity, although done with the permission of the Law, was not done with his permission, so it depends on his ratification. The ruling is general and includes after the item has perished, because ownership of the poor person is established before ratification, so it does not require the item's existence, unlike the sale of a third party (fuduli) where the validity of ratification requires the item's existence because ownership is established after ratification. As for holding the finder liable, it is because he handed over the owner's property to someone else without the owner's permission, though it was permissible by the Law; this is not incompatible with liability for the rights of the servant, as in the case of consuming another's property during dire necessity. This ruling includes the case where the charity was done by order of the qadi, which is the correct view, because the qadi's order is not higher than his own act; if the qadi himself had given it in charity, he could hold himself liable, so he can hold liable the one who ordered the qadi. Therefore the author used the word 'finder' generically to include the qadi. The author did not mention the liability of the poor person; they said: he has a choice: if he wishes, he holds the finder liable, and if he wishes, he holds the poor person liable. Whichever he holds liable does not have recourse against the other. If he holds the finder liable, the finder owns the item from the time of taking and gets the reward. If the item is still extant, he takes it from the hand of the poor person; as in al-Khaniyah, and from this it is known that the reward is suspended.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage explains a key provision in the law of luqatah: when the finder has given the found item in charity, and later the owner appears, the owner has the option to either ratify the charity (thus the charity is valid and the finder gets the reward) or to claim compensation from the finder (i.e., recover the value of the item). The rationale is that the charity, though permitted by Islamic law, was done without the owner's consent; so it is contingent upon his approval. The passage also notes that this option applies even if the charity was done by order of the qadi, because the qadi's authority does not override the owner's right. Furthermore, the owner may also hold the poor recipient liable, but if he does so, the finder becomes the owner of the item from the time of taking and is entitled to the reward. This clarifies the complex interplay between the finder's charitable act and the owner's property rights.

Key Terms
الملتقط (al-multaquit): The finder of the luqatah
التصدق (al-tasadduq): Giving in charity the found item after proper announcement
الضمان (al-daman): Liability for compensation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the owner of a luqatah appears after the finder has given it in charity, what choices does the owner have?
A) He can only force the finder to compensate him.
B) He can only let the charity stand and get the reward.
C) He can choose to either enforce the charity and get the reward, or hold the finder liable for compensation.
D) He can do nothing because the charity was done legally.

Key Takeaway
The charity of luqatah by the finder is valid only with the owner's subsequent ratification; without it, the owner can demand compensation from the finder.

The Hanafi View on Picking Up Lost Animals: Dispute with Other Schools
Ruling and difference of opinion on picking up lost animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on picking up a lost animal, identify the disagreement with the three imams, and explain the contextual reasoning for preferring to take it.

Core Idea
Taking a lost animal is recommended today to safeguard property, despite a hadith advising to leave it, because times have changed.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi position on picking up a lost animal (bahima). It clarifies that the ruling is mandub (recommended) and not obligatory, except when loss is feared. The text also addresses a famous hadith about a lost camel and shows how the Hanafi school contextualizes it to a time of widespread trustworthiness, making taking the animal the better option in our time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is picking up a lost animal. The Hanafi ruling is that it is valid (sahih) in the sense of being recommended (mandub).
2
Locate the opposing evidence
The hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari where the Prophet (ﷺ) told the questioner to leave the camel alone because it can fend for itself.
Hadith: 'Leave it, for it has its sandals and its water skin...'
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
From Al-Mabsut: the hadith was specific to a time when people were trustworthy; in our time, the animal may be stolen, so taking it becomes a form of preserving property.
Hanafi reasoning: istihsan based on changed circumstances.
4
Understand the scope of ikhtilaf
The disagreement with the other three imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) is only about recommending the act; all agree it is permissible.
No one says it is haram.
5
Note the exception
If there is fear of loss, it becomes necessary (wajib) to take it; otherwise, it is merely recommended.
Condition: fear of loss (khiyafat al-daya')

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وصح التقاط البهيمة) أي ندب التقاطها لأنها يتوهم ضياعها فيستحب أخذها وتعريفها صيانة لأموال الناس، وأما ما في الصحيح حين سئل عن ضالة الإبل قال مالك ولها معها حذاؤها وسقاؤها ترد الماء وتأكل الشجر فذرها حتى يجدها ربها فأجاب عنه في المبسوط بأن ذلك كان إذ ذاك لغلبة أهل الصلاح والأمانة لا تصل إليها يد خائنة فإذا تركها وجدها وأما في زماننا فلا يأمن من وصول يد خائنة إليها بعده ففي أخذها إحياؤها. وإنما فسرنا الصحة بالندب لأن خلاف الأئمة الثلاثة إنما هو في ندب التقاطها فإنهم قالوا تركها أفضل لا أنهم قالوا بعدم الجواز وإنما يكون مندوباً عندنا إذا لم يخف الضياع وإلا لم يسعه تركه ؛ كذا في الولوالجية. قال: ولا فرق عندنا أن تكون البهيمة في القرية أو في الصحراء، ومحل الاختلاف الثاني.
His statement: 'The picking up of the animal is valid' means its picking up is recommended (nadb). Because it is feared that it may be lost, so it is recommended to take it and announce it as a preservation of people's property. As for what is in Sahih (al-Bukhari) when he was asked about a lost camel, he said: 'Leave it, for it has its sandals and its water skin, it comes to water and eats leaves, so leave it until its owner finds it.' The response in al-Mabsut is that this was at that time when there was a prevalence of righteous and trustworthy people, and no treacherous hand would reach it. So if left, the owner would find it. But in our time, one cannot feel safe from a treacherous hand reaching it afterwards, so taking it is reviving it. We have interpreted 'valid' as 'recommended' because the disagreement of the three imams is only about recommending its taking: they said leaving it is better, not that it is not permissible. It is recommended in our view if there is no fear of loss; otherwise, it is not permissible to leave it. As in al-Walwaljiyya. He said: There is no difference in our view whether the animal is in a village or in the desert. And the second point of disagreement.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Hanafi ruling on picking up a lost animal (bahima). The author explains that the statement 'it is valid' means it is recommended (mandub), not that it is mandatory or merely permissible. The Hanafi position is that it is recommended to take the animal and announce it to safeguard people's property, despite the hadith that seems to indicate leaving it. The reason is that the hadith applied to a time of widespread trustworthiness, whereas in later times, taking it is a form of preserving property. The three other imams (presumably Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar, or the three other schools) actually say leaving it is better, not that it is forbidden. So the disagreement is about recommendation, not permissibility. The passage also states that there is no difference between village and desert. This is the second point of disagreement (the first being the recommendation itself).

Key Terms
البهيمة (al-bahima): A lost animal, typically a camel, cow, etc., that can fend for itself
ضالة الإبل (daallat al-ibil): A lost camel; the hadith specifically mentions this
ندب (nadb): Recommended act (equivalent to mustahabb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on picking up a lost animal in current times?
A) It is haram because it belongs to someone else.
B) It is mandatory (fard).
C) It is recommended (mandub) unless there is fear of loss, in which case it becomes necessary.
D) It is permissible but not recommended.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on picking up lost animals is recommended in normal times and necessary when loss is feared, based on contextualizing a prophetic hadith.
